BULLET POINTS
View binding is a type-safe way of interacting with views. It’s
more efficient than using findViewById(), and less likely
to produce errors at runtime.
View binding can be used in activity and fragment code.
Enable view binding in the app’s build.gradle file.
View binding generates a binding class for each of the app’s
layout files.
Each binding class includes a property for each view in the
layout with an ID.
When you use view binding with an activity, you set its
binding property in the activity’s onCreate() method.
When you use view binding with a fragment, you set its
binding property in the fragment’s onCreateView()
method, and set it to null in onDestroyView().
Fragments have lifecycle methods that are linked to an
activity’s state.
Chapter 11. View Models: Model
Behavior
As apps grow more complex, fragments have more to juggle.
And if you’re not careful, this can lead to bloated code that tries to do
everything. Business logic, navigation, controlling the UI, dealing with
configuration changes…you name it, it’s in there. In this chapter, you’ll
learn how to deal with this kind of situation using view models. You’ll
discover how they simplify your activity and fragment code. You’ll find
out how they survive configuration changes, keeping your app’s state safe
and sound. Finally, we’ll show you how to build a view model factory, and
when this might be needed.
Configuration changes revisited
As you learned in Chapter 5, Bad Things can happen when you rotate the
screen while running an app. Changing the screen orientation is a
configuration change, which causes Android to destroy and recreate the
current activity. As a result, views and properties can lose their state and get
reset:
In Chapter 5, you learned how to handle this using the activity’s
onSaveInstanceState method. This method gets triggered before the
activity gets destroyed, and it’s used to save any values that may get lost in
a Bundle. When the activity gets recreated, you can use these saved values
to restore the state of the activity’s views and properties.
NOTE
Fragments have an onSaveInstanceState method too. Even though we’ve only shown
you how to use this approach with an activity, it works with fragments as well.
Using a Bundle to save state works well for relatively simple apps, but it’s
not an ideal solution for more complicated ones. This is because Bundles
are only intended to hold a small amount of data, and only work with a
limited number of types.
There are other problems, too
Another problem apps can have is that the activity and fragment code can
quickly become bloated. The code may need to control navigation, update
the UI, save state, and also include more general business logic to control
the app’s behavior. Having all of this in one place makes the code longer,
which can then be harder to read and maintain.
In this chapter we’re going to learn how to solve all of these problems using
a view model.
Introducing the view model
A view model is a separate class that sits alongside the activity or fragment
code. It’s responsible for all of the data that needs to be displayed on the
screen, along with any business logic. Whenever a fragment needs to update
its layout, it asks the view model for the latest values it needs to display,
and if it needs access to some business logic, it calls the methods held in the
view model.
Why use a view model?
There are a couple reasons why you might want to use a view model.
Using a view model simplifies your activity or fragment code. Your
fragment no longer needs to include code relating to the app’s business
logic as this is all held in a separate class. Instead, it can focus on things
like updating the screen or navigation.
Another reason is that the view model can survive configuration changes.
It doesn’t get destroyed when the user rotates the device screen, so the state
of any variables doesn’t get lost. This lets you restore the state of your app
without having having to store values in a Bundle.
We’re going to find out how to use view models by building a word
guessing game. Before we start writing the code, let’s go through how the
game will work.
THERE ARE NO DUMB QUESTIONS
Q: You said Bundles are only intended to hold a limited number of
data types. Does that mean there’s no way of adding extra types?
A: Technically, you can add extra types to a Bundle using an interface
named Parcelable. We’re not teaching you this approach because
we think it’s better to use a view model.
If you’re feeling curious, however, you can find out more about using
Parcelable in the Android documentation.
What the guessing game will do
The aim of the Guessing Game app is for the user to try and guess a secret
word.
When the game starts, it chooses a word at random from an array, and
displays a blank for each of the word’s letters:
The user suggests a letter that she thinks is in the secret word. If she guesses
correctly, the game displays the letter where it appears in the secret word. If
she guesses incorrectly, the game displays the incorrect guess, and she loses
a life.
The user continues to make guesses until she finds all of the letters, or runs
out of lives. When this happens, a new screen appears, telling her what the
secret word was, and whether she won or lost the game. She’s given the
option to start another game.
How the app will be structured
The app will contain a single activity (named MainActivity), which
will be used to display the game’s fragments: GameFragment and
ResultFragment.
GameFragment is the game’s main screen. It will display blanks for the
secret word’s letters, let the user make guesses, and show any wrong
choices and the number of lives left.
When the game is over, GameFragment will use the Navigation
component to navigate to ResultFragment, passing it a String for the
result. ResultFragment will display the String and a button to let the
user start a new game.
We’ll go into more details about how the app is structured when we build it.
First, let’s run through the steps we’ll take.
Here’s what we’re going to do
Here are the steps that we’ll go through to write the app:
1. Write the basic game.
We’ll create GameFragment and ResultFragment, write the
game logic, and use the Navigation component to navigate
between the two fragments.
2. Add a view model for GameFragment.
We’ll create a view model, GameViewModel, to hold
GameFragment’s game logic and data. This will simplify the
GameFragment code, and ensure that the game survives
configuration changes.
3. Add a view model for ResultFragment.
We’ll add a second view model, ResultViewModel, for
ResultFragment to use. This view model will hold the result
of the game that the user’s just played.

How navigation should work
As you know, a navigation graph tells Android about an app’s possible
destinations, and how to navigate to them.
In the Guessing Game app, we want navigation to work as follows:
1. When the app gets launched, display GameFragment.
2. When a game is won or lost, GameFragment navigates to
ResultFragment, passing it the result.
3. When the user clicks on the New Game button, navigate to
GameFragment.
To implement this, we’ll add GameFragment and ResultFragment to
a new navigation graph (which we’ll create), and specify that each fragment
can navigate to the other.
We’ll also say that GameFragment will pass a String argument to
ResultFragment, specifying a message that indicates whether the user
has won or lost the game they’ve just played. ResultFragment will
display this message when it’s navigated to.

Let’s go through what happens when the code runs, and take the app for a
test drive.
What happens when the app runs
The following things happen when the app runs:
1. The app is launched and GameFragment is displayed in
MainActivity.
GameFragment sets livesLeft to 8, correctGuesses and
incorrectGuesses to "", secretWord to a word it chooses
at random, and secretWordDisplay to the value of
deriveSecretWordDisplay(). It then calls its
updateScreen() method, which displays the values of
livesLeft, incorrectGuesses, and
secretWordDisplay.
2. When the user makes a guess, GameFragment calls its
makeGuess() method.
The method checks if secretWord contains the letter the user
has guessed. If it does, makeGuess() adds the letter to
correctGuesses and updates secretWordDisplay. If it
doesn’t, the method adds the letter to incorrectGuesses, and
subtracts 1 from livesLeft. It then calls updateScreen()
again so that the new values are displayed.
3. After each guess, GameFragment checks if isWon() or isLost()
is true.
These methods check if the user has guessed all the letters in the
word, or if she has run out of lives. If either method returns true,
GameFragment passes the result to ResultFragment, which
displays the result.
Test Drive
When we run the app, GameFragment is displayed. It shows how many
letters the secret word has, and how many lives we have.
We can make a guess by entering a letter in the edit text, and clicking the
button. If we guess correctly, it places the letter in the secret word, but if we
get it wrong, we lose a life.
If we guess all the letters or run out of lives, a message telling us whether
we’ve won or lost is displayed in ResultFragment.
The game seems to work, but what happens if we rotate the screen?
The game loses state when the screen
rotates
There’s a problem with the game, however. If we rotate the screen when
we’re in the middle of a game, the app loses its state and the game starts
from the beginning.
The game loses its state because rotating the screen changes the app’s
configuration, so Android destroys the activity (and the fragment it
displays) and immediately recreates it. This resets the game’s views and
properties.
We could solve this problem using the fragment’s
onSaveInstanceState method to save the state of any properties, as
we did earlier in the book. This time, however, we’re going to fix it by
implementing a view model instead.
A view model holds business logic
As we said earlier, a view model is a separate class that sits alongside your
activity or fragment code. It’s responsible for the data that needs to be
displayed on the screen along with any business logic. In our Guessing
Game app, for example, this means that the view model needs to hold the
game’s properties—like the secret word the user needs to guess, and the
number of lives left—and any methods that control how the game is played.
When you implement a view model, all of the code relating to the app’s
data or business logic is moved out of the activity or fragment, and into the
view model. Any code that controls the UI—such as displaying text, or
getting user input—stays in the activity or fragment code.
This way of architecting Android apps follows a design principle known as
separation of concerns. The app is split into different classes, where each
class addresses a separate concern. A UI controller—the activity or
fragment code—handles the UI, and the view model is responsible for the
business logic and data:
Use a view model to simplify your activity and fragment code, and hold
the state of any properties so that they survive configuration changes.
Using this sort of app architecture has two key advantages.
It simplifies your activity and fragment code.
Moving the app’s data and business logic into a view model means
that there’s less activity and fragment code for you to maintain.
Your app survives configuration changes.
The view model is a separate class that sits alongside your activity
or fragment code. It’s not destroyed when you rotate the device
screen, so the state of any properties held in the view model
survives configuration changes without you having to add their
values to a Bundle.
Now that you’ve learned what using a view model buys you, let’s see how
to add one to the Guessing Game app.

Create a GameViewModel object
To link a view model to an activity or fragment, you need to add a
ViewModel property to the code, and initialize it with a ViewModel
object, which you need to create. The code looks like this:
As you can see, the above code uses a view model provider to create the
ViewModel object. So what does using this class do?
Use a ViewModelProvider to create view models
As its name suggests, ViewModelProvider is a special class whose job
is to provide activities and fragments with view models. It ensures that a
new view model object only gets created if one doesn’t already exist.
As you already know, when the screen is rotated, any fragments that are
displayed on the screen get destroyed and recreated. When this happens, the
view model provider makes sure that the same view model object continues
to be used. The view model keeps its state, so any properties that are used
by the fragment don’t get reset.
The view model provider keeps hold of the view model so long as the
activity or fragment stays alive. When a fragment is detached or removed
from its activity, for example, the view model provider lets go of the
fragment’s view model. The next time the view model provider is asked to
provide a view model object, it creates a new one.
Now that you’ve learned how to link a view model to a fragment, let’s
update the GameFragment code.

What happens when the app runs
The following things happen when the app runs:
1. GameFragment asks the ViewModelProvider class for an

The view model provider sees that there’s no existing
GameViewModel object associated with the fragment, so it
creates a new one.
2. The GameViewModel object is initialized.
It sets livesLeft to 8, correctGuesses and
incorrectGuesses to "", secretWord to a word it chooses
at random, and secretWordDisplay to
deriveSecretWordDisplay().
3. GameFragment calls its updateScreen() method.
The method accesses the GameViewModel object’s
secretWordDisplay, livesLeft, and
incorrectGueses properties, and shows them on the screen.
4. When the user makes a guess, GameFragment calls the
GameViewModel object’s makeGuess() method.
The method checks if secretWord contains the letter the user
guesses. If it does, it adds the letter to correctGuesses and
updates secretWordDisplay. If it doesn’t, it adds it to
incorrectGuesses, and subtracts 1 from livesLeft.
5. GameFragment calls its updateScreen() method again.
The method gets the updated property values from the
GameViewModel object, and updates the screen.
6. After each guess, GameFragment checks if the view model’s
isWon() or isLost() methods return true.
If either method returns true, GameFragment passes the result to
ResultFragment, which displays the result.
Test Drive

playing the game and rotate the screen, the game retains its state.
You’ve now learned how to add a view model to your apps, and used it to
avoid the problems that can occur when the user rotates the device screen.
Before we go any further, let’s take a closer look at view models.
VIEW MODELS UP CLOSE
A view model is a separate class you write that’s responsible for a UI
controller’s business logic and data. It extends the
androidx.lifecycle.ViewModel class, and may optionally
override a method named onCleared(). This method gets called just
before the view model gets cleared away, and it gives you a chance to
dispose of any resources:
NOTE
The UI controller can be an activity or fragment.
import androidx.lifecycle.ViewModel
class MyViewModel : ViewModel() {
 //Code to initialize the view model
 override fun onCleared() {
 //Code that runs when the view model is about to get
cleared away
 }
}
You create a view model object using the ViewModelProvider
class, for example:
viewModel =
ViewModelProvider(this).get(MyViewModel::class.java)
This creates a view model in the same scope as the UI controller. It
ensures that a fresh view model only gets created if there’s not an
existing one in the UI controller’s scope. This means that the view
model survives configuration changes, so you don’t have to save the
state of any instance variables in a separate Bundle.
The view model stays alive until its UI controller permanently goes
away. If it’s linked to an activity, this is when the activity has finished:
when it’s been destroyed and won’t be recreated. If it’s linked to a
fragment, it’s when the fragment has been removed or detached from its
parent activity.
You can track when a view model has been created or cleared away
using the android.util.Log class. This class lets you add
messages to Android’s log (or logcat) that you can examine in Android
Studio. The following code, for example, logs a message when the view
model is cleared:
The Log class includes several methods you can use to log different
types of message. The Log.i() method lets you log information, for
example, while Log.e() is used for recording errors.
Here is a list of the different Log methods you can use, and what
they’re used for:
Log.v(String tag, String message) Logs a verbose message.
Log.d(String tag, String message) Logs a debug message.
Log.i(String tag, String message) Logs an information message.
Log.w(String tag, String message) Logs a warning message.
Log.e(String tag, String message) Logs an error message.
NOTE
There’s also a Log.wtf() method you can use to report conditions that should
never happen. According to the Android documentation, wtf means “What a
Terrible Failure.” We know it really means “Welcome to Fiskidagurinn,” which
refers to the Great Fish Day festival held annually in Dalvik, Iceland. Android
developers can often be heard to say, “My virtual device just took 8 minutes to
boot up. WTF??” as a tribute to the small town that gave its name to the standard
Android executable bytecode format.
Each message is composed of a String tag you use to identify the
source of the message, and the message itself. So the code:
Log.i("MyViewModel", "ViewModel cleared")
logs an information message that’s come from MyViewModel.
You can view the logcat in Android Studio by choosing the Logcat
option at the bottom of the screen. This lets you filter by the different
types of message, and search for text:
BE the View Model
The code below describes a view model class named MyViewModel.
Your job is to play like you’re the view model, and say what problems
there are with this code and how they could be fixed.
package com.hfad.myapp
import androidx.lifecycle.ViewModel
import android.util.Log
import android.widget.TextView
class MyViewModel {
 val num = 2
 init {
 Log.i("MyViewModel", "ViewModel created")
 }
 override fun onCleared() {
 Log.i("MyViewModel", "ViewModel cleared")
 }
 fun calculation(val1: Int, val2: Int): Int {
 Log.i("MyViewModel", "Called Calculation")
 return (val1 + val2) * num
 }
 fun joinTogether(text1: TextView, text2: TextView): String {
 Log.i("MyViewModel", "Called JoinTogether")
 return ("${text1.text} ${text2.text}")
 }
}

Earlier, we were able to add a GameViewModel reference to

This tells the view model provider to get the GameViewModel object

The above approach, however, can’t be used to add a ResultViewModel

A view model factory creates view models
An alternative way of creating a view model is to pass the view model
provider a view model factory: a separate class whose sole purpose is to
create and initialize view models. This approach means that the view model
provider doesn’t have to worry about creating a view model by itself.
Instead, it uses the view model factory.
While view model factories can be used for any sort of view model, they’re
mostly used for ones whose constructors require arguments. This is because
the view model provider can’t pass arguments to the constructor by itself: it
needs a view model factory to do so.
Here’s how the view model factory will be used in the Guessing Game app:
Use a view model factory to create a view model that doesn’t have a noargument constructor.
1. We’ll define a class named ResultViewModelFactory, which
ResultFragment will use to create a factory object.
2. ResultFragment will tell the view model provider to use the
factory object.
3. When the view model provider needs a new ResultViewModel
object, it will use the ResultViewModelFactory.
Let’s go ahead and add a factory class to the Guessing Game app.

object.

Use the factory to create the view model

passing the factory to the view model provider. The view model provider
decides when a new view model object is required, and when necessary,
uses the factory to create one.

After some questions, we’ll go through what happens when the app runs.
THERE ARE NO DUMB QUESTIONS
Q: Does ResultFragment really need its own view model?
A: We decided to use one so that we could separate out any game logic
and data required by ResultFragment. This is only a result
String in the current app, but it’s good practice in case the app gets
more complicated. And let’s face it, it was a good excuse for us to teach
you about using view model factories.
Q: Why didn’t we use a factory when we created
GameViewModel?
A: Factories are only necessary when you need to create a view model
that doesn’t have a no-arguments constructor. As GameViewModel
uses the default constructor which has no arguments, we didn’t need to
use a factory.
Q: Could we still have used a factory for GameViewModel if we’d
wanted to?
A: Yes. You can use factories to create any sort of view model, but if
the view model has a no-argument constructor, you don’t need to use
one.
Q: Why did we need to add a constructor to ResultViewModel?
A: So that we could set the result String as soon as the view model
was created. This guarantees that the result String has a value
whenever ResultFragment uses the view model.
Q: Do you have to create view models using the
ViewModelProvider class? What happens if you don’t?
A: Yes, you should always use the view model provider to create a view
model object. The view model provider only creates a new view model
object if one doesn’t already exist for the UI controller’s scope, which
means that the view model survives configuration changes.
If you instantiate a view model without using the view model provider,
it will get recreated each time the UI controller is destroyed and
recreated. The view model would lose its state each time the device
screen is rotated.
Q: Can you unit test view models?
A: Yes! View models don’t refer to any of the screen’s views, so they
can be unit tested independently of activities and fragments. You don’t
have to run the app to test them.
Q: Why didn’t we create a view model for MainActivity?
A: In the Guessing Game app, MainActivity is only used to display
the game’s fragments. As MainActivity doesn’t include any game
logic or data, we didn’t need to create a view model for it.
Q: Can two fragments share the same view model?
A: If you have two fragments in an activity that need to communicate
with each other, you might want to add a single view model to the
activity that the two fragments then have access to. You can find out
more about this here:
https://developer.android.com/topic/libraries/architecture/viewmodel
What happens when the app runs
The following things happen when the app runs:
1. GameFragment asks the ViewModelProvider class for an
instance of GameViewModel.
The GameViewModel object is initialized, and chooses a word at
random.
2. GameFragment interacts with the GameViewModel object.
The GameViewModel object records any guesses the user has
made, and keeps track of the number of lives left.
3. After each guess, GameFragment checks if the view model’s
isWon() or isLost() methods return true.
If either method is true, GameFragment navigates to
ResultFragment, passing it the result.
4. ResultFragment creates a ResultViewModelFactory object,
passing it the result String.
5. ResultFragment asks the ViewModelProvider class for an
instance of ResultViewModel.
The ViewModelProvider class sees that there’s no existing
ResultViewModel object, so it uses the
ResultViewModelFactory to create one. The
ResultViewModel object’s result property is initialized
with the result String.
6. ResultFragment gets the result String from the
ResultViewModel object, and displays it on the screen.

ResultFragment. A message is displayed telling us whether we won or
lost, and what the secret word was.
The game behaves in the same way as it did before, but behind the scenes,
it’s now using view models for its game logic and data.

BULLET POINTS
A view model simplifies your activity or fragment code by
separating out any code relating to its business logic and data.
View models can survive configuration changes.
A view model is usually associated with a single UI controller.
The view model extends the class
androidx.lifecycle.ViewModel.
Create a view model by calling the ViewModelProvider
class get() method. This ensures that a view model is only
created where one doesn’t already exist.
A view model stays alive until its UI controller permanently
goes away. This is when the activity is finished, or the
fragment is removed from its activity.
If a view model’s constructor requires arguments, you need to
write an additional factory class for it. The
ViewModelProvider class uses the factory each time it
needs to create an instance of the view model.
The view model factory class must implement an interface
named ViewModelProvider.Factory.
Chapter 12. Live Data: Leaping
into Action
Your code often needs to react to property value changes.
If a view model property changes value, for example, the fragment might
need to respond by updating its views or navigating elsewhere. But how
does a fragment get to hear when a property’s been updated? Here,
we’ll introduce you to live data: a way of telling interested parties when
something’s changed. You’ll learn all about MutableLiveData, and how to
make your fragment observe properties of this type. You’ll discover how
the LiveData type helps maintain your app’s integrity. Soon, you’ll be
writing apps that are more responsive than ever before...
The Guessing Game app revisited
In the previous chapter, we built a Guessing Game app that lets the user
guess which letters are included in a secret word. When the user guesses all
the letters or runs out of lives, the game ends.
To stop the fragment code from becoming too bloated and maintain the
app’s state when the user rotated the device screen, we used view models
for the app’s game logic and data. GameFragment uses
GameViewModel for its logic and data, and ResultViewModel holds
the game result that’s needed by ResultFragment:
When each fragment is displayed, or the user makes a guess, the fragments
get the latest values from their view models and display them on the screen.
While this approach works, however, there are some disadvantages.
The fragments decide when to update views
The downside to this approach is that each fragment decides when to fetch
the latest property values from the view model and update its views. And
some of the time, these values won’t have changed. If the user makes a
correct guess, for example, GameFragment updates the text that’s
displayed for the number of lives left and the incorrect guesses, even though
these values won’t have changed.
Let the view model say when a value’s changed
An alternative approach is for GameViewModel to let GameFragment
know when each of its properties has been updated. If the fragment is
notified of these changes, it will no longer need to decide for itself when to
fetch the latest property values from the view model and update its views.
Instead, it will only need to update its views once it’s told that the
underlying property has been updated.
We’re going to implement this change in the Guessing Game app using
Android’s live data library: part of Android Jetpack. Live data lets the view
model tell interested parties—such as fragments and activities—when its
property values have been updated. They can then react to these changes by
updating views, or calling other methods.
You’ll find out how to use live data through the rest of this chapter. First,
let’s go through the steps we’ll take to update the app.
Here’s what we’re going to do
Here are the steps that we’ll go through to write the app:
1. Make the Guessing Game app use live data.
We’ll update GameViewModel so that the livesLeft,
incorrectGuesses, and secretWordDisplay properties
use live data. We’ll then make GameFragment update its views
when the values of these properties change.
2. Protect GameViewModel’s properties and methods.
We’ll restrict access to GameViewModel’s properties so that only
GameViewModel can update them. We’ll also make sure that
GameFragment only has access to the methods it requires to do
its job.
3. Add a gameOver property.
We’ll make GameViewModel decide when each game is over
using a new gameOver property. GameFragment will navigate
to ResultFragment when the value of this property changes.
Add a live data dependency to the app build.gradle file
As we’re going to be using live data, we’ll begin by adding a live data
dependency to the app’s build.gradle file.
Open the project for the Guessing Game app (if you haven’t already done
so), open the file GuessingGame/app/build.gradle, and add the following
line (in bold) to the dependencies section:
When prompted, sync your changes.
GameViewModel and GameFragment need to
use live data
We want to use live data in the Guessing Game app so that
GameViewModel notifies GameFragment when its property values
have changed. GameFragment will then react to these changes.
We’ll tackle this in two stages:
1. Specify which GameViewModel property changes
GameFragment needs to know about.
2. Tell GameFragment how to respond to each change.
For now, we’ll focus on the GameViewModel code changes.
Which view model properties should use live data?
GameViewModel includes three properties—secretWordDisplay,
incorrectGuesses, and livesLeft—that GameFragment uses to
update its views. We’ll specify that these three properties use live data so
that GameFragment is notified when their values change.
You specify that a property uses live data by changing its type to
MutableLiveData<Type>, where Type is the type of data the
property should hold. The livesLeft property, for example, is currently
defined as an Int using the code:
var livesLeft = 8
To make the property use live data, you change its type to
MutableLiveData<Int> so that it looks like this instead:
This specifies that livesLeft is now a MutableLiveData<Int>
with an initial value of 8.
Similarly, we can define the incorrectGuesses and
secretWordDisplay properties using code like this
Here, each property’s type is set to MutableLiveData<String>.
incorrectGuesses’s value is set to "", while
secretWordDisplay’s value will get set in GameViewModel’s init
block.
So that’s how you define a live data property. Next up, how to update its
value.
Live data objects use a value property
When you use MutableLiveData properties, you update their values
using a property named value. To update the secretWordDisplay
property with the deriveSecretWordDisplay() method’s return
value, for example, you don’t use the code:
secretWordDisplay = deriveSecretWordDisplay()
as you did before. You use the following code instead:
secretWordDisplay.value = deriveSecretWordDisplay()
Changing the value property in this way is important, because it’s how
any interested parties—in this case GameFragment—get notified of any
changes. Each time secretWordDisplay’s value property gets
updated, GameFragment is informed, so it can respond by updating its
views.
The value property can be null
There’s one extra thing to be aware of when you use live data: the value’s
type is nullable. This means that when you use live data values in your
code, you need to perform null-safety checks, or your code won’t compile.
As an example, the livesLeft property is defined using the code:
This property’s type is MutableLiveData<Int>, so its value
property can accept an Int, or be null.
As the value property may be null, we can’t, say subtract one from its
value using the code:
livesLeft.value--
Instead, we need to use:
which subtracts 1 from its value so long as it isn’t null.
Similarly, the following isLost() method won’t compile because
liveLeft.value may be null:
fun isLost() = livesLeft.value <= 0
We can, however, change it to use Kotlin’s Elvis operator like this:
Live Data properties can be defined using val.
As you already know, you use val and var in Kotlin to specify whether or
not a property can be given a reference to a new object.
When we first defined each property, we used var so that we could update
it. We defined the livesLeft property, for example, using:
var livesLeft = 8
which initialized livesLeft with an Int object whose value is 8. Each
time the user made an incorrect guess, we subtracted 1 from livesLeft,
which gave it a reference to a new Int object:
With live data, you update the value property of the existing object,
instead of replacing it with another object, and interested parties are notified
of this change. As the object is no longer being replaced, you can define the
property using val instead of var like this:

The fragment observes the view model
properties and reacts to changes.
You make a fragment respond to value changes in a view model’s
MutableLiveData property by calling the property’s observe()
method. If the following code is added to GameFragment, for example, it
will observe the view model’s livesLeft property, and take action when
it changes:
As you can see, the above code passes viewLifecycleOwner and
Observer arguments to the observe() method.
viewLifecycleOwner refers to the lifecycle of the fragment’s views.
It’s tied to when the fragment has access to its UI: from when it’s created in
the fragment’s onCreateView() method, to when its destroyed, and
onDestroyView() gets called.
Observer is a class that can receive live data. It’s tied to the
viewLifecycleOwner, so it’s only active—and able to receive live data
notifications—when the fragment has access to its views. If the live data
property’s value changes while the fragment doesn’t have access to its UI,
the observer isn’t notified, so the fragment doesn’t respond. This stops the
fragment from trying to update views when they’re not available, which
might cause the app to crash.
NOTE
It also makes your coding life easier because you don’t need to check whether the
view’s available for yourself. Using live data handles it all for you.
The Observer class accepts a lambda parameter, which specifies how the
property’s new value should be used. In the Guessing Game app, for
example, we want GameFragment to update its lives text each time the
view model’s livesLeft property gets updated, and we can achieve this
using the following code:
That’s everything that we need to know in order to make GameFragment
update its views when the view model property values get changed. Let’s
update its code.

through what happens when the app runs.
ResultViewModel doesn’t need to use live data, so we don’t need to
update it.
As you may recall, ResultViewModel has one property (named
result), which gets set when the view model gets created. The code
looks like this:
class ResultViewModel(finalResult: String) : ViewModel(){
 val result = finalResult
}
As you can see, result is defined using val, so once it’s been initialized, it
can’t be updated to another value. ResultFragment doesn’t need to be
notified when result changes because once set, result can never
change. There’s no need to make ResultFragment respond to any
changes, because there can’t be any.
So let’s walk through what happens when the code runs, and then take the
app for a test drive.
THERE ARE NO DUMB QUESTIONS
Q: What happens if GameFragment is inactive when one of
GameViewModel’s live data properties gets updated? Won’t this
make the app crash?
A: No. Live data is lifecycle-aware, so it only notifies active lifecycle
observers when its property values have changed. If GameFragment
is inactive when GameViewModel’s properties get updated,
GameFragment won’t get notified, so you don’t have to worry about
the app crashing.
Q: Is there a LiveData type too?
A: Yes! The LiveData type is like MutableLiveData except that
its value property can’t be updated. You’ll find out how the
LiveData type is used a few pages ahead.
What happens when the app runs
The following things happen when the app runs:
1. GameFragment asks the ViewModelProvider class for an
instance of GameViewModel.
The GameViewModel object is initialized, and the values of its
three MutableLiveData properties—livesLeft,
incorrectGuesses, and secretWordDisplay—are set.
2. GameFragment observes the livesLeft, incorrectGuesses, and
secretWordDisplay properties in the GameViewModel.
3. GameFragment updates its views with the values of the
properties it’s observing.
4. When the user makes a correct guess, secretWordDisplay’s
value is updated, and the new value is passed to
GameFragment.
GameFragment responds by updating its word view, which is
displayed on the screen.
5. When the user makes an incorrect guess, the values for
incorrectGuesses and livesLeft are updated, and passed to
GameFragment.
GameFragment responds by updating its views.
6. When isWon() or isLost() return true, GameFragment
navigates to ResultFragment, passing it the result.
ResultFragment displays the result.
Let’s take the app for a test drive.
Test Drive
When we run the app, GameFragment is displayed as before.
When we make a correct guess, the secret word display gets updated. When
we make an incorrect guess, the lives left is updated, and our guess is added
to the incorrect guesses display.
If we guess all the letters or lose all our lives, the app navigates to
ResultFragment, which displays the result.
The game behaves in the same way as before, but behind the scenes, it
responds using live data.
SHARPEN YOUR PENCIL
The code below describes a view model named MyViewModel. See if
you can change the code so that it uses live data to let fragments
respond to the rate property changing.
package com.hfad.myapp
import androidx.lifecycle.ViewModel
import android.util.Log
class MyViewModel : ViewModel() {
 var rate = 2
 init {
 Log.i("MyViewModel", "ViewModel created")
 }
 override fun onCleared() {
 Log.i("MyViewModel", "ViewModel cleared")
 }
 fun updateRate(newRate: Int) {
 rate = newRate
 }
 fun Calculation(val1: Int, val2: Int): Int {
 Log.i("MyViewModel", "Called Calculation")
 return (val1 + val2) * rate
 }
 fun JoinTogether(text1: String, text2: String): String {
 Log.i("MyViewModel", "Called JoinTogether")
 return ("${text1} ${text2}")
 }
}
SHARPEN YOUR PENCIL SOLUTION
The code below describes a view model named MyViewModel. See if
you can change the code so that it uses live data to let fragments
respond to the rate property changing.
Fragments can update GameViewModel’s
properties
So far, we’ve updated the code in GameViewModel and
GameFragment so that it uses live data. Each time the value of a
MutableLiveData property in GameViewModel gets updated,
GameFragment responds by updating its views.
There’s a slight problem with the code, however. GameFragment has full
access to GameViewModel’s properties and methods, so if it wanted to, it
could use them inappropriately. There’s nothing to stop the fragment from,
say, updating the livesLeft property to 100 so that the user can make
many more guesses and win the game each time she plays.
To get around this problem, we’re going to restrict direct access to the
GameViewModel’s properties so that only methods in the view model
can update them.
You can restrict direct access to a view model’s property by marking it
private, and providing read-only access via another property’s getter.
Keep your privates private
To protect GameViewModel’s properties, we’ll mark each one as
private so that only code in GameViewModel can update their values.
We’ll then expose a read-only version of each MutableLiveData
property that GameFragment needs to observe. Instead of defining the
livesLeft property using code like this:
val livesLeft = MutableLiveData<Int>(8)
we’ll use the following:
Here, the _livesLeft property holds a reference to a
MutableLiveData object. GameFragment doesn’t have access to this
property because it’s marked as private.
GameFragment can, however, access the value of this property using
livesLeft’s getter. livesLeft’s type is LiveData, which is like
MutableLiveData except that it can’t be used to update the underlying
object’s value property: GamesFragment can read the value, but it
can’t update it.
When you structure your code in this way, the private property is sometimes
called a backing property. It holds a reference to an object that other
classes only have access to via another property.
Let’s update the GameViewModel code.
The full code for GameViewModel.kt
We’ll update the code for GameViewModel so that it uses backing
properties to restrict direct access to its live data properties. We’ll also mark
as private any properties and methods that GameFragment doesn’t need
to use.

changes (in bold):

What happens when the app runs
The following things happen when the app runs:
1. GameFragment asks the ViewModelProvider class for an
instance of GameViewModel.
2. GameViewModel’s properties are initialized.
livesLeft, incorrectGuesses, and
secretWordDisplay are LiveData properties that refer to
the same underlying objects as their MutableLiveData backing
properties.
3. GameFragment observes the livesLeft, incorrectGuesses, and
secretWordDisplay properties.
GameFragment can’t update any of these properties, but it
responds when GameViewModel updates any of the backing
properties because they refer to the same underlying object.
4. GameFragment continues to respond to value changes until
isWon() or isLost() returns true.
GameFragment navigates to ResultFragment, passing it the
result. ResultFragment displays the result.

We’ve nearly finished updating the Guessing Game app. There’s just one
more thing we need to change...
GameFragment still includes game logic
In the current version of the app, GameFragment decides when the game
is finished by calling GameViewModel’s isWon() and isLost()
methods after the user has made each guess. If either of these returns true,
GameFragment navigates to ResultFragment, passing it the result.
Here’s the current code:
The problem with this approach is that GameFragment decides when the
game is over instead of GameViewModel. As determining when the game
has finished is a game decision, it’s something that GameViewModel
should be responsible for, not GameFragment.
Make GameViewModel decide when the game is over
To deal with this, we’ll add a MutableLiveData<Boolean> property
named _gameOver to GameViewModel, whose value we’ll expose
using a LiveData property named gameOver. We’ll set this property to
true when the user wins or loses the game. When this happens,
GameFragment will respond by navigating to ResultFragment.
You’re already familiar with the changes that are required to do this, so
before we show you the code, try the following exercise.

What happens when the app runs
The following things happen when the app runs:
1. GameFragment asks the ViewModelProvider class for an
instance of GameViewModel.
2. GameViewModel’s properties are initialized.
The _gameOver and gameOver properties refer to a
MutableLiveData<Boolean> object whose value is set to
false.
3. GameFragment observes the GameViewModel’s gameOver
property.
GameFragment can’t update the MutableLiveData object
that the gameOver property refers to, but it can respond to when
its value changes.
4. Each time the GameViewModel’s makeGuess() method is
called, it checks whether isWon() or isLost() returns true.
If either is true, it sets the value of its _gameOver property to
true.
5. GameFragment observes the value has been updated to true
via the GameViewModel’s gameOver property.
The new value is passed to GameFragment.
6. GameFragment responds by navigating to ResultFragment,
passing it the result.

Your Android Toolbox
You’ve got Chapter 12 under your belt and now you’ve added live data

BULLET POINTS
Live data lets the view model’s properties tell interested parties
(such as fragments and activities) when its values have
changed.
To make a property use live data, define the property using
val, and change its type to MutableLiveData<Type>
where Type is the type of data the property needs to hold.
The MutableLiveData object’s value property is used to
hold its value. This property is nullable.
You make a fragment or activity respond to changes in the
view model by calling the property’s observe method and
specifying how the new value should be used.
You can restrict direct access to a view model’s properties
using backing properties.
The LiveData type is like MutableLiveData except that
it’s immutable. It’s used to expose a MutableLiveData
object’s value while preventing it from being updated.
Chapter 13. Data Binding:
Building Smart Layouts
Layouts can do more than control your app’s appearance.
All of the layouts you’ve written so far have been told how they should
behave by activity or fragment code. But just imagine if the layouts could
think for themselves, and make their own decisions. In this chapter, we’ll
introduce you to data binding: a way of boosting your layout’s IQ. You’ll
find out how to make views get values directly from the view model.
You’ll use listener binding to make buttons call their methods. You’ll even
discover how one simple line of code lets views respond to live data
updates. Soon, your layouts will be more powerful than ever before.
Back to the Guessing Game app
In the previous two chapters, you built a Guessing Game app that lets the
user guess which letters are included in a secret word. When the user
guesses all the letters or runs out of lives, the game ends.
The app uses two view models—GameViewModel and
ResultViewModel—which hold the app’s game logic and data, and
maintain its state when the app is rotated. GameViewModel is used by
GameFragment, and ResultViewModel is used by
ResultFragment:
The first screen the user sees is GameFragment, and she interacts with it
to play the game. It displays information—such as the number of lives left
and any incorrect guesses the user has made—and lets the user make
guesses.
When the game is over, the app navigates to ResultFragment. This
screen tells the user whether she has won or lost the game, and what the
secret word was.
The fragments update the views in their
layouts
Each of the app’s fragments are responsible for updating the views in their
layouts. ResultFragment, for example, sets the text in its won_lost
text view to the value of ResultViewModel’s result property:
But while this approach works, it has a downside. When you make
fragments responsible for keeping views up-to-date in this way, it can make
your Kotlin code more complicated, and harder to read and maintain.
So what’s the alternative?
We can make the views update themselves instead
The alternative is to use a technique called data binding. With data
binding, views can get their values directly from the view model, so the
fragment code no longer has to keep them updated. Instead of using code in
ResultFragment to update the text in the won_lost text view, for
example, we can use data binding to make the view get its text directly from
ResultViewModel’s result property.
In this chapter, you’ll learn how to use data binding by implementing it in
the Guessing Game app. Let’s go through the steps we’ll take to do this.
Here’s what we’re going to do
Here are the steps that we’ll go through to make the Guessing Game app
use data binding:
1. Implement data binding for ResultFragment.
We’ll update ResultFragment so that its layout uses data
binding to display the value of the result property directly from
ResultViewModel.
2. Implement data binding for GameFragment.
We’ll then change up GameFragment so that its layout uses data
binding to display property values from GameViewModel. Along
the way, we’ll also make the layout dynamically respond to any
live data updates.
3. Add a Finish Game button to GameFragment.
Finally, we’ll add a new button to GameFragment’s layout that,
when clicked, immediately finishes the game.
Let’s get started.
Enable data binding in the app build.gradle
file
Just like with view binding, you need to enable data binding in the
android section of the app’s build.gradle file before you can use it in
your app. The code to enable data binding looks like this:

enable data binding too. Open the file GuessingGame/app/build.gradle, and
update the buildFeatures section so that it looks like this:

project.

ResultFragment.
THERE ARE NO DUMB QUESTIONS
Q: I see that in the Guessing Game app, both view binding and data
binding are enabled. Do you always need to enable view binding in
order to use data binding?
A: No, you don’t. It’s quite common to enable data binding without
enabling view binding, and you’ll find out more about this later in the
chapter.
For now, all you really need to know is that we’re keeping the view
binding option enabled so that all the app’s existing code continues to
work.
ResultFragment updates the text in its layout
As you may recall, ResultFragment’s layout contains a text view with
an ID of won_lost, which is defined like this:
When the app navigates to ResultFragment, the fragment gets the
value of the result property from its view model, and displays it in the
text view using this code:
We’re going to change the fragment and its layout so that it uses data
binding. Instead of getting ResultFragment to update the text view,
we’ll make the text view get its value directly from the view model.
How we’ll implement data binding
To make the text view get its text from ResultViewModel, we need to
update the code for ResultFragment and its layout. We’ll do this by
going through the following steps:
1. Add <layout> and <data> elements to the layout.
The <layout> element is required for data binding, and the
<data> element is used to set up a data binding variable that links
the layout to the view model.
2. Set the layout’s data binding variable to an instance of the view
model.
We’ll do this using fragment code.
3. Use the data binding variable to access the view model’s
properties.
We’ll update the won_lost text view so that it gets its text value
directly from the view model.
Let’s begin by updating the layout code.
1. Add <layout> and <data> elements
Every layout that uses data binding takes the following form:
The above layout has a <layout> element as its root element. This
enables data binding in the layout, so it’s required by all layouts that need to
use data binding.
Within the <layout> element sits a <data> element. This is used to
specify any variables—defined using the <variable> element—that are
needed for data binding, such as the view model you want the layout’s
views to gets their data from. The above code, for example, uses:
to specify a data binding variable named resultViewModel whose type
is ResultViewModel.
The <layout> element also includes a view or view group for the layout’s
view hierarchy. If you want to display views in a linear layout, for example,
you’d put the <LinearLayout> element and its views inside the
<layout> element along with all of its views. You’ll see how this is done
a few pages ahead.
Once you’ve defined a data binding variable, you need to set it. We’ll see

THERE ARE NO DUMB QUESTIONS
Q: All of the other elements we’ve added to layouts, like
<LinearLayout> and <Button>, have all had an initial capital
letter. Why are <layout>, <data>, and <variable> all
lowercase?
A: All of the other elements you’ve learned about so far have had initial
capital letters because they are class names for types of View. The
<LinearLayout> element, for example, refers to the
LinearLayout class found in the android.view package.
<layout>, <data>, and <variable> are all lowercase because
they’re instructions, not Views. When Android sees the <variable>
element, for example, it treats it as an instruction to create a variable.

object.

model’s properties and methods.

<LAYOUT> UP CLOSE
As we said earlier, every layout that uses data binding must have a
<layout> element as its root element like this:
<layout ... >
 ...
</layout>
When you enable data binding in an app’s build.gradle file, it generates
a binding class for each layout with a <layout> element at its root.
This is the same binding class that gets generated when you enable
view binding.
In the Guessing Game app, the layout file fragment_result.xml has a
<layout> element as its root element. This means that its binding
class FragmentResultBinding will still get generated by data
binding even if view binding is switched off:
View binding and data binding generate the same binding classes,
but in different situations.
You add one or more data binding variables to the layout using the
<data> and <variable> elements:
View binding generates a binding class for every layout, while data
binding generates one for every layout with a <layout> element.
You set each variable to an object with a matching type in Kotlin code.
Once set, the layout’s views can use the data binding variable to access
its object’s properties and methods.

We need to set the gameViewModel variable
The first change we need to make to the GameFragment code is to set the
layout’s data binding variable gameViewModel to an instance of the
fragment’s view model.
GameFragment currently includes the following line in its
onCreateView() method, which uses the view model provider to get a
GameViewModel object:
viewModel =
ViewModelProvider(this).get(GameViewModel::class.java)
We can therefore assign this view model to the gameViewModel data
binding variable by adding the following line:
binding.gameViewModel = viewModel
Once the gameViewModel variable is linked to the view model in this
way, the layout’s views can use it to get their values.
We also need to make the data binding use live data
As well as setting the gameViewModel variable, we need to make the
layout’s data binding work with live data.
Unlike ResultViewModel, the GameViewModel code uses live data
so that when its property values are updated, the GameFragment code
responds. The following code in GameFragment.kt, for example, observes
the incorrectGuesses property, and updates the
incorrect_guesses view when it’s updated:
viewModel.incorrectGuesses.observe(viewLifecycleOwner, Observer {
newValue ->
 binding.incorrectGuesses.text = "Incorrect guesses:
$newValue"
})
As we’re now using data binding, we can make each view respond to live
data changes by adding the following line to the fragment code:
binding.lifecycleOwner = viewLifecycleOwner
Each time a live data property value in the view model gets updated, the
layout no longer has to rely on the fragment code updating its views. The
layout can respond to any updates without any further intervention from the
fragment.
That’s everything that we need to know in order to update the
GameFragment code. Let’s see what it looks like.

What happens when the app runs
The following things happen when the app runs:
1. GameFragment gets a reference to its GameViewModel object.
2. GameFragment assigns the GameViewModel object to the
layout’s gameViewModel data binding variable.
The layout’s views can now use this variable to access the view
model’s properties and methods.
3. GameFragment sets the layout’s lifecycle owner.
The layout can now observe the view model’s live data properties
and respond to any changes.
4. When the user makes a correct guess, the GameViewModel
object’s secretWordDisplay property gets updated.
The layout observes this change and its word view displays the
new text.
5. When the user makes an incorrect guess, the GameViewModel
object’s livesLeft and incorrectGuesses properties are updated.
The layout observes the changes and its corresponding views
display the new text.
6. When the game is over, GameFragment navigates to
ResultFragment.

THERE ARE NO DUMB QUESTIONS
Q: Can you use data binding to make views update properties in
the view model?
A: Yes! Views can update mutable properties using @= instead of @.
We’ll show you an example of this in the next chapter.
Q: You’re setting the word view’s text to
"@{gameViewModel.secretWordDisplay}". As
secretWordDisplay‘s type is LiveData, shouldn’t you use
secretWordDisplay.value instead?
A: No. The layout will automatically use the value property without
you telling it to.
Q: I tried to set the text of the lives view to
"@{gameViewModel.livesLeft}" but the app crashed. Any
idea why?
A: The livesLeft.value property is an Int?, and the view’s
text property needs to be a String. Try converting the property to a
String using:
"@{gameViewModel.livesLeft.toString()}" That should
stop the app from crashing.

You can use data binding to call methods
So far, you’ve implemented data binding so that views get their values
directly from the view model instead of relying on fragment code.
Another way in which you can use data binding is to make a button call a
method in the view model without writing any extra fragment code. To see
how this works, we’re going to add a new Finish Game button to
GameFragment’s layout that, when clicked, will finish the game.
Here’s what the new version of the screen will look like:
To make the new button finish the game, we’ll do the following:
1. Add a new finishGame() method to GameViewModel.
This method will set the view model’s _gameOver property to
true. When this happens, the existing code in GameFragment
will respond by navigating to ResultFragment.
2. Add the new button to the layout and make it call
finishGame().
The button will call the method using data binding.
We’ll begin by updating the GameViewModel code.
Add finishGame() to GameViewModel.kt
We need to add a new finishGame() method to the GameViewModel
code that sets the value of _gameOver to true.
You already know how to do this, so here’s the new code for
GameViewModel.kt; make sure that you update the code to include the new
method (in bold):
That’s all the GameViewModel code we need to add. Next, we’ll add the
new Finish Game button to GameFragment’s layout, and make it call the
finishGame() method.
Use data binding to make a button call a
method when clicked
As we said earlier, you can use data binding to make a button call a method
in its view model without writing any extra fragment code. To do this, you
add an android:onClick attribute to the button, and set its value to an
expression that calls the method.
In the Guessing Game app, we want to add a new button to
GameFragment’s layout that calls GameViewModel’s
finishGame() method when clicked. The code for this button looks like
this:
The line:
android:onClick="@{() -> gameViewModel.finishGame()}"
is just like adding the following code to your fragment:
binding.finishGameButton.setOnClickListener() {
 viewModel.finishGame()
}
except that this time, no fragment code is required. Behind the scenes, data
binding uses the line:
android:onClick="@{() -> gameViewModel.finishGame()}"
to create an onClickListener for the button. When the user clicks the
button, the code is executed, which calls the view model’s
finishGame() method.
When you use a binding expression to call a method such as:
"@{() -> gameViewModel.finishGame()}"
the binding expression is sometimes known as a listener binding.
That’s everything that we need to know in order to add the button to
GameFragment’s layout and make it respond to clicks. Let’s see what the
full code looks like.

out more here:
https://developer.android.com/topic/libraries/data-binding/expressions

Fireside Chats
Tonight’s talk: View Binding and Data Binding air their differences
Data Binding: View Binding:
View Binding, we need to talk.
We do? Why?
Surely you’ve noticed how people keep
getting the two of us confused. I mean, just
because we both have Binding in our names,
it doesn’t mean we’re the same thing.
You’ve got to admit our names sound similar.
Besides, we have a lot in common.
You think so?
Yes! We both generate binding classes. The same
binding classes. Don’t pretend that’s not true.
Maybe we do, but under completely different
circumstances.
It’s true that I’m much more convenient and helpful
than you. I generate a binding class for every layout,
and most of the time, that’s exactly what’s needed.
Developers thank me for it. I even get fan mail. “Oh,
View Binding, I’m your biggest fan! Thank you for
eliminating findViewById. I thought my pain
would never end!”
You can’t claim sole responsibility for getting
rid of findViewById. That’s down to the
binding class, not you. The binding class that
I generate, too.
Whatever.
Besides, generating a binding class for every
single layout is such a waste of space.
Strictly speaking, I ignore any layouts with a view
BindingIgnore attribute that’s set to true. That’s
way less complicated than your approach.
Complicated? I’m just more discerning than
you are. I only generate binding classes for
layouts with a <layout> element. That
way, the developer gets to decide exactly
where I’m needed.
Yes, complicated! Who really wants to add even
more XML to their layout code? That’s so much
extra typing!
So it means a bit of extra code, but I can do
things that you can only dream of. I let views
get their values directly from the view model,
for instance.
I can do that too. Using fragment code.
But that’s the point. You rely on fragment
code to tell the views what they should do,
but when I’m involved, that’s not necessary. I
let the views get the values directly, which
means simpler fragment code. I even let them
use live data properties so that they can keep
themselves up to date.
Huh. But what about updating property values? I bet
you still need fragment code for that.
No, I don’t. I have two-way data binding
where views can update values and respond
to changes. Look it up. It’ll change your life.
Huh. You’ll be telling me next you let views call
methods.
But of course. And why not?
It makes the layout code overly complicated. I just
can’t believe you’d be so irresponsible.
I hardly call simple method calls
irresponsible, and it saves the developer from
writing yet more fragment code. But I accept
that for more complex things, the code really
belongs in the fragment, not the layout.
So even you admit you have your limitations.
You’re just jealous.
Riiiiiight. You keep telling yourself that…
We can switch off view binding
We’ve nearly finished updating the Guessing Game app, but there’s one
more tweak we can make so that only the binding classes we’re using for
data binding will get generated.
As you know, data binding and view binding generate the same binding
classes, but under different circumstances: view binding generates a binding
class for every layout, while data binding generates the class for every
layout with a <layout> element at its root.
In the Guessing Game app, we’re only using the binding classes for
fragment_game.xml and fragment_result.xml. Since both of these files
include a <layout> element, we can safely switch off the view binding
option, and their binding classes will continue to get generated via data
binding:
NOTE
The only layout file that data binding won’t generate a binding class for is
activity_main.xml because it doesn’t include a <layout> element. We don’t need to use
this binding class, so view binding can be safely switched off.
Switch off view binding in the app’s build.gradle file
To switch off view binding, open the GuessingGame/app/build.gradle file,
and remove the view binding line from the buildFeatures section like
this:
Then choose the Sync Now option to sync this change with the rest of the
project.

Switching off view binding has made no difference because data binding
has generated all the binding classes that the app needs.

BULLET POINTS
Data binding gives layouts direct access to properties and
methods.
Enable data binding in the app’s build.gradle file.
Add a <layout> element to the root of each layout that needs
to use data binding.

<layout> element. This is the same binding class that gets
generated with view binding.

Appendix A. Leftovers: The Top
Ten Things (we didn’t cover)
Even after all that, there’s still a little more.
There are just a few more things we think you need to know. We wouldn’t
feel right about ignoring them, and we really wanted to give you a book
you’d be able to lift without extensive training at the local gym. Before you
put down the book, read through these tidbits.
1. Sharing data with other apps
If you want to share simple data with another app, you can do so with an
Intent. You can think of an Intent as an “intent to do something.” It’s
a type of message that lets you send data to another object—such as an
activity—at runtime.
Sharing data with Android’s intent resolver
If you want to pass text to another activity, you can do so using code like
this:
val sendIntent: Intent = Intent().apply {
 action = Intent.ACTION_SEND
 putExtra(Intent.EXTRA_TEXT, "This is some text.")
 type = "text/plain"
}
startActivity(sendIntent)
The code first creates an Intent named sendIntent. It uses the type
property to specify the type of data that’s being sent (in this case plain text),
and uses putExtra() to attach the data (here, it’s some text).
The action property tells Android which types of activity can receive the
intent. Here, it’s set using:
action = Intent.ACTION_SEND
which means that only activities that can send messages can receive the
intent.
The intent is sent using the line:
startActivity(sendIntent)
Behind the scenes, Android looks for activities across all the device’s apps
that can accept intents with the specified action and type. If it finds multiple
such activities, it displays an Intent Resolver screen, which lets the user
choose which app they want to share the data with.
Android then starts the activity, passing it the data.
Sharing data with the Android Sharesheet
In most cases, you’ll want to share data using the Android Sharesheet
instead. This lets you specify who you want to share data with and how, and
the code looks like this:
As you can see, the code includes an extra call to the Intent’s
createChooser() method. When this runs, it displays the Android
Sharesheet like this:
You can find out more about sharing data with other apps—and how to
make your app receive data—here:
https://developer.android.com/training/sharing
2. WorkManager
There are times when you’ll want your app to process data in the
background. This might be because it needs to access storage, for example,
or it needs to download a large file.
As you learned in Chapter 14, you can use Kotlin coroutines for tasks that
need to be executed immediately. But what if you want to defer the task, or
have a long-running task that must continue to run, even if the device
restarts?
Use WorkManager to schedule deferrable tasks
If you want to schedule a task to run in the background, you can use
Android’s WorkManager API. It’s part of Android Jetpack, and it’s
designed for potentially long-running tasks that are guaranteed to run even
if the user quits the app or restarts the device.
You can even use WorkManager to run tasks when certain constraints are
met, such as when WiFi is available, or chain complex tasks together.
You can find out more about WorkManager—and how to use it—here:
https://developer.android.com/topic/libraries/architecture/workmanager
3. Dialogs and notifications
In Chapter 9, you learned how to show the user a simple pop-up message
using a toast and a snackbar. These are useful for low priority messages that
appear in the app, and don’t require any user action.
There are times, however, when you need to display a message that prompts
the user to make a decision, or that’s displayed outside the app’s UI. In
these situations, you can use dialogs and notifications.
Use dialogs to prompt the user for a decision
A dialog is a small window that appears in the middle of the screen.
They’re normally used for situations where the user must make a decision
before the app can proceed:
You can find out how to create and use dialogs here:
https://developer.android.com/guide/topics/ui/dialogs
Notifications appear outside the app’s UI
If you want to remind the user to do something, or tell them when they’ve
received a message, you can use a notification. Notifications appear on the
device status bar and the notification drawer, and may appear on the device
lock screen as well:
You can find out how to create and use notifications here:
https://developer.android.com/guide/topics/ui/notifiers/notifications
4. Automated testing
If you create an app that’s intended to be used by thousands or even
millions of people, you will quickly lose users if it’s flaky or keeps
crashing. You can prevent many of these problems, however, using
automated testing.
Two popular testing frameworks are JUnit and Espresso. When you create a
new Android project, Android Studio usually includes dependencies for
these in the app’s build.gradle file.
Automated testing usually falls into two categories: unit tests and
instrumented tests.
Unit tests
Unit tests run on the development machine, and they check the individual
pieces—or units—of your code. They live in the app/src/test folder of your
project, and look something like this:
package com.hfad.myapp
import org.junit.Test
import org.junit.Assert.*
class ExampleUnitTest {
 @Test
 fun additionIsCorrect() {
 assertEquals(6, 3 + 3)
 }
}
Instrumented tests
Instrumented—or on-device—tests run inside an emulator or physical
device, and check the fully assembled app. They live in the
app/src/androidTest folder of your project.
We’ll show you an example of an instrumented test on the next page.
An instrumented test example
Here’s an instrumented test that checks whether a composable displays the
correct text:
package com.hfad.myapp
import androidx.compose.material.MaterialTheme
import androidx.compose.material.Surface
import androidx.compose.ui.test.junit4.createComposeRule
import androidx.compose.ui.test.onNodeWithText
import androidx.test.ext.junit.runners.AndroidJUnit4
import org.junit.Rule
import org.junit.Test
import org.junit.runner.RunWith
@RunWith(AndroidJUnit4::class)
class HelloTest {
 @get:Rule
 val composeTestRule = createComposeRule()
 @Test
 fun shouldShowHello() {
 composeTestRule.setContent {
 MaterialTheme {
 Surface {
 Hello("Fred")
 }
 }
 }
 composeTestRule.onNodeWithText("Hello
Fred!").assertExists()
 }
}
Go here to find out more about using automated testing with Android apps:
https://developer.android.com/training/testing
5. Supporting different screen sizes
Android apps come in all sorts of shapes and sizes, and you want your apps
to look good on all of them. There are several techniques you can use,
including constraint layouts (which you learned about in Chapter 4),
providing alternate layouts, and sliding pane layouts.
Provide alternate layouts
Android apps can have multiple versions of the same layout file that cater to
different screen specs. This is done by creating multiple layout folders, each
one with an appropriate name, and adding a separate layout file to each one.
To have one layout for devices with a width of 600dp or above (such as 7”
tablets), and another for smaller devices, add an extra folder to the
app/src/main/res folder named layout-sw600dp. Then put a layout for wider
devices to use in the layout-sw600dp folder, and one for smaller devices in
the layout folder.
When the app runs on a phone, it will use the layout in the layout folder as
normal, and when it runs on a wider device, it will use the one in the layoutsw600dp folder instead.
You can find out more about using width qualifiers here:
https://developer.android.com/training/multiscreen/screensizes#alternativelayouts
Use a SlidingPaneLayout
Some layouts include a navigable list of records, where clicking on an item
displays its details. On small devices, you might want the details to appear
in a separate screen, but on large devices, you might prefer to display the
list and details side by side.
To deal with this kind of situation, you can use a SlidingPaneLayout
to define separate panes for the list and details. The code looks like this:
The layout uses the layout-width attributes of each pane to figure out
whether they’ll fit side by side on the device. If the device is wide enough,
the layout displays them next to one another, and if it’s not, it displays them
on separate screens.
You can find out more here:
https://developer.android.com/reference/androidx/slidingpanelayout/widget/
SlidingPaneLayout
6. More Compose features
In Chapter 18 and Chapter 19, we introduced you to building UIs with
Compose. We think it has a bright future ahead of it, but it’s so new that at
the time of writing, some of its libraries aren’t yet stable.
We didn’t want to leave without giving you a glimpse into the future, so
here are some of the libraries and features we’re most excited about.
The Compose ViewModel library
In Chapter 19, we passed an existing view model to a composable so it
could access the view model’s properties and methods. If you use the
Compose view model library, this is no longer necessary; the composable
can get the view model for itself.
Add the following dependency to the app’s build.gradle file:
implementation "androidx.lifecycle:lifecycle-viewmodelcompose:1.0.0-alpha07"
Then add code like this to the composable:
Compose constraint layout library
In Chapter 18, we showed you how to arrange composables in rows and
columns. If you need something a little more flexible, however, there’s the
Compose constraint layout library. As you might expect, this library lets
you position composables using constraints.
You can find out more about this library, and how to use it, here:
https://developer.android.com/jetpack/compose/layouts/constraintlayout
The Compose Navigation component
As you already know, you navigate between screens in a View-based UI
using the Navigation component. You define a separate fragment for each
screen, and the Navigation component decides which fragment to display.
When you use the Compose Navigation component, you no longer need to
use fragments. Instead, you define a composable for each screen, and the
Navigation component decides which one to display.
To use the Compose Navigation component, add the following dependency
to the app’s build.gradle file:
implementation("androidx.navigation:navigation-compose:2.4.0-
alpha08")
