You can keep up-to-date with Compose here:
https://developer.android.com/jetpack/compose
7. Retrofit
In Chapter 14, you learned how to persist your data in a Room database
held on the device. But what if your data is held remotely instead?
In this situation, you can use Retrofit. This is a third-party REST client for
Android, Java, and Kotlin that lets you make network requests, interact with
REST APIs, and download JSON or XML data.
You can find out more about Retrofit here:
https://square.github.io/retrofit/
You can also find out more about including Retrofit in your app’s
architecture by going here:
https://developer.android.com/jetpack/guide
8. Android Game Development Kit
If you’re interested in developing games for Android apps, we suggest you
check out the Android Game Development Kit (AGDK). This is a range of
libraries and tools that let you develop, optimize, and deliver Android
games.
The Android Game Development Kit includes game libraries for C/C++
game development. There’s a GameActivity, for example, which
inherits from AppCompatActivity and uses a C API. There’s also a
way of using the software keyboard in C, and handling input from game
controllers.
NOTE
[Note from Marketing: Make sure you mention your book Head First C.]
You can read more about the Android Game Development Kit here:
https://developer.android.com/games/agdk
9. CameraX
If you want to use the device camera in your app, there’s a Jetpack library
you can use called CameraX. This gives you a consistent API that works
across most Android devices, and resolves device-compatibility issues. You
can use it to preview, capture, and analyze images. There’s even an
Extensions add-on that lets you access features used by the native camera
app that ships with the device.
Read more about CameraX here:
https://developer.android.com/training/camerax
10. Publishing your app
Once you’ve developed your app, you’ll probably want to make it available
to other users. You’ll likely want to do this by publishing your app through
an app marketplace such as Google Play.
There are two stages to this process: preparing your app for release, and
then releasing it.
Preparing your app for release
Before you can release your app, you need to configure, build, and test a
release version of it. This includes tasks such as deciding on an icon for
your app, removing any logging code, and modifying AndroidManifest.xml
so that only devices you want to be able to run your app can download it.
Before you release your app, make sure that you test it on at least one tablet
and one phone to check that it looks the way you expect and its
performance is acceptable.
You can find further details of how to prepare your app for release here:
http://developer.android.com/tools/publishing/preparing.html
Releasing your app
This stage includes publishing your app, selling it, and publicizing it.
Before you publish your app we suggest you go here:
https://developer.android.com/distribute/best-practices/launch
It includes checklists and tips to help you publish and manage your app.
For ideas on how to best target your app to your users and build a buzz
about it, we suggest you explore the documents here:
https://developer.android.com/distribute/best-practices/engage
and here:
https://developer.android.com/distribute/best-practices/grow
Chapter 14. Room Databases:
Room with a View
Most apps need data that persists.
But if you don’t take steps to store this data somewhere, it will be lost
forever as soon as the app is closed down. You usually keep data safe in
Androidville by storing it in a database, so in this chapter, we’ll introduce
you to the Room persistence library. You’ll learn how to build databases,
create tables, and define data access methods using annotated classes and
interfaces. You’ll find out how to use coroutines to run database code in
the background. And along the way, you’ll discover how to transform
your live data as soon as it changes with a little help from
Transformations.map().
Most apps need to store data
Nearly all of the apps that you’ve written so far have used a small amount
of static data in order to function. The Guessing Game app we built in
Chapter 11 to Chapter 13, for example, holds an array of Strings in its
view model so that the game can select one at random for you to guess.
In the real world, however, most apps require more than just static data;
they need to be able to save data that can change so that it doesn’t get lost
when the user shuts down the app. A music app might need to store
playlists, for example, and a game might need to record the user’s progress
so that they can go back to where they left off.
Apps can persist data using a database
In most cases, the best way of persisting the user’s data is to use a database,
so in this chapter, you’re going to learn how to use one by building a Tasks
app. This app will let the user add tasks to a database, and display a list of
all the tasks they have already entered.
Here’s what the app will look like:
Before we start building the app, let’s run through how it will be structured.
How the app will be structured
The app will contain a single activity (named MainActivity), which
will be used to display a fragment named TasksFragment.
TasksFragment is the app’s main screen. Its layout file
(fragment_tasks.xml) will include an edit text and a button, which let the
user enter a task name and insert it into the database. It will also feature a
text view, which will display all of the tasks that have been entered into the
database:
We’ll also add a view model (named TasksViewModel) to the app,
which TasksFragment will use for its business logic. It will include
properties and methods that the fragment will use to interact with the app’s
database. We’ll also enable data binding so that TasksFragment’s layout
has direct access to the view model.
Here’s how these components will interact:
To create the database, we’re going to use an Android library called Room.
So what’s Room?
Room is a database library that sits on top of
SQLite
Behind the scenes, most Android databases use SQLite. SQLite is
lightweight, stable, fast, and optimized for a single user, and these features
make it a good choice for Android apps. Writing the code to create,
manage, and interact with SQLite databases, however, can be tricky.
To make things easier, Android Jetpack includes a persistence library
named Room that sits on top of SQLite. With Room, you get all the benefits
of using SQLite, but using simpler code. It provides you with convenient
annotations, for example, that let you quickly write database code in a way
that’s less repetitive and less error-prone.
MVVM is an architectural design pattern that’s used to structure apps.
It stands for Model-View-ViewModel.
Room apps are usually structured using MVVM
Apps that use Room—including the Tasks app—are usually structured
using an architectural design pattern called MVVM, which stands for
Model-View-ViewModel. The structure looks like this:
This structure is similar to the one we used for the Guessing Game app we
built, except that there’s an extra Model layer for the database. It means that
the activity and fragment’s UI code is cleanly separated from the business
logic that’s held in the view model, and the view model is separated from
any code that underpins the database.
You’ll learn more about how to use the MVVM structure as we build the
Tasks app.
Here’s what we’re going to do

1. Set up the basic app.
We’ll create the app, update its build.gradle files so that it uses the
libraries we need, and create the basic activity, fragment, and
layout code.
2. Write the database code.
In this step, we’ll add the code to create a database with a table,
along with the data access methods needed to interact with the
table’s data.
3. Insert task records.
We’ll create a view model and update the app’s fragment so that
we can use the app to insert records.
4. Display a list of task records.
Finally, we’ll update the view model and fragment code so that the
app displays a list of all the task records held in the database.

Now that we’ve updated the fragment and activity code, let’s get to work on
the Room database.
How Room databases are created
Room uses a set of annotated classes and interfaces to create and configure
a SQLite database for your app. It requires three main things:
1. A database class
This defines the database, including its name and version number. It’s used
to get an instance of the database.
2. Data classes for the tables
All of the data in a database is stored in tables. You define each table using
a data class, which includes annotations to specify the table’s name and
columns.
NOTE
A Kotlin data class lets you create objects whose main purpose is to hold data.
3. Interfaces for data access
You interact with each table using an interface, which specifies the data
access methods your app needs. If you need to insert records, for example,
you can add an insert() method to the interface, and to get all records,
you can add a getAll() method.
Room uses these three things to generate all the code your app needs to
create the SQLite database, its tables, and any data access methods.
Over the next few pages, we’re going to show you how to write the code for
these three components by defining a database for the Tasks app. We’ll
begin by defining its table.
We’ll store tasks data in a table
As we said before, all of a database’s data is stored in one or more tables.
Each table is made up of rows and columns, where each row is a record,
and each column holds a single piece of data, like a number or piece of text.
You create a separate table for each type of data you want to hold. A
calendar app, for example, might have a table to record events, and a
weather app might include a table for its locations.
We want to store task records in the Tasks app, so we’re going to create a
table named “task_table”. The table will look something like this:
You define tables with an annotated data class
You specify any tables you want the database to include by defining a data
class for each table. The data class needs to include a property for each of
the table’s columns, and you use annotations to tell Room how the table
should be configured.
To see how this works, we’re going to define a data class named Task,
which we’ll use to create a table in the Tasks app’s database.
Create the Task data class
We’ll begin by creating the data class. Highlight the com.hfad.tasks package
in the app/src/main/java folder, then go to File→New→Kotlin Class/File.
Name the file “Task” and choose the Class option.
When you’ve created the file, update its code so that it looks like this:

Now that we’ve created the Task data class, we need to add annotations to

specifying the table name. We want to give the table in the Tasks app a

Specify the primary key with @PrimaryKey
Next, we’ll specify the table’s primary key. This is used to uniquely identify

like this:
Note that the @ColumnInfo annotation is only needed if you want the
column name to be different to the property name. If you omit the
annotation, Room will give the column the same name as the property.

THERE ARE NO DUMB QUESTIONS
Q: If I leave out the @ColumnInfo annotation, will Room still
create a column for that data class property?
A: Yes. It will create a column with the same name as the property. The
@ColumnInfo annotation simply lets you rename the column.
Q: Can I stop Room from creating a column for one of the data
class properties?
A: Yes. If you don’t want to persist the data for one of the properties in
the data class, you can annotate it with @Ignore. When you do so,
Room won’t create a column in the table for this property.
Use an interface to specify data operations
You specify how the app will access the table’s data by creating an
annotated interface. This interface defines a DAO—or Data Access Object
—which includes all the methods the app needs to insert, read, update, and
delete the data.
To see how this works, we’ll create a new interface named TaskDao,
which the Tasks app will use to interact with task_table’s data.
Create the TaskDao interface
We’ll begin by creating the interface. Highlight the com.hfad.tasks package
in the app/src/main/java folder, then go to File→New→Kotlin Class/File.
Name the file “TaskDao” and choose the Interface option.
When you’ve created the file, make sure that its code looks like this:
Use @Dao to mark the interface for data access
Next, we need to tell Room that the TaskDao interface defines data access
methods. This is done by marking the interface with an @Dao annotation
like this:
Once you’ve annotated the interface with @Dao, you add annotated
methods that the app will use to interact with the data. If you want the app
to insert records, for example, you need to add a method for this to the
interface, and if you want it to get records, you need to add this method too.
The great news is that Room provides four annotations—@Insert,
@Update, @Delete, and @Query—that make adding these methods a
breeze. Let’s find out how these are used by adding some data access
methods to TaskDao.
Use @Insert to insert a record
The first data access method we’ll define is an insert() method, which
the app will use to insert a task into task_table. The method will have
one parameter—a Task—for the task we want to insert. We’ll also mark
the method with an @Insert annotation, which tells Room that the
method is used to insert records.

the code the app needs to insert a record into the table so that you don’t
have to write it yourself. For the insert() method above, for example, it
generates all the code needed to insert a Task object’s data into
task_table like this:

for their arguments—objects whose type is marked with @Entity.

existing records in the table. This is done by adding a method to the DAO

Use @Query for everything else
Any other data access methods are marked with @Query. This annotation
lets you define a SQL statement (using SELECT, INSERT, UPDATE, or
DELETE) that will be run when the method is called.

We’re not going to teach you how to use SQL in this book, but if you want to learn
more, we suggest reading Head First SQL by Lynn Beighley.

Next, we’ll learn how to define the actual database.
Create a TaskDatabase abstract class
You define the app’s database by creating an abstract class. The abstract
class specifies the database name and version number, and any classes or
interfaces that define the tables and data access methods.
In the Tasks app, we’re going to define the database using an abstract class
named TaskDatabase. Highlight the com.hfad.tasks package in the
app/src/main/java folder, then go to File→New→Kotlin Class/File. Name
the file “TaskDatabase” and choose the Class option.
The TaskDatabase class needs to extend RoomDatabase, so update
the code for TaskDatabase.kt so that it looks like this:
Annotate the class with @Database
Next, we need to mark the class with @Database, which tells Room that it
defines a database. Here’s the code to do this:
As you can see, the @Database annotation includes three attributes:
entities, version, and exportSchema.
entities specifies any classes—marked with @Entity—that define the
tables you want Room to add to the database. For the Tasks app, this is the
Task data class.
version is an Int that specifies the database version. In this case, it’s 1,
as this is the first version of the database.
Finally, exportSchema tells Room whether to export the database
schema into a folder so that you can record its version history. Here, we’re
setting it to false.
WATCH IT!
If you modify the database entity files, you need to update the
version number.
If you change the schema without updating the version number, the app
will crash when you try to run it.

Before TasksFragment can call the addTask() method, however,

Database operations can run in slooooowmoooo
Some tasks in Androidville, such as inserting records into a database, are
potentially quite time-consuming. For this reason, the Room persistence
library insists that any data access operations must be performed on a
background thread so they don’t block Android’s main thread and hold up
the UI.
NOTE
Technically, there’s a setting you can use to override this, but it’s way better to run tasks
such as this on a background thread so they don’t hold up the rest of the app.
As we’re developing Android apps using Kotlin, we’re going to use
coroutines to make sure all of TaskDao’s data access methods run in the
background.
We’ll use coroutines to run data access code in the
background
As you may already know, a coroutine is like a lightweight thread that lets
you run multiple pieces of code asynchronously. Using coroutines means
that you can launch a background job—such as inserting records into a
database—without the rest of the code having to wait around for it to
complete. This gives your user a more fluid experience, so it’s not like
trying to watch YouTube on Thanksgiving.
Changing your data access code to use coroutines is pretty straightforward.
You simply have to make these two changes:
A coroutine is a piece of suspendable code that can be run in the
background.
1. Mark each of the DAO’s data access methods with suspend.
This turns each method into a coroutine that’s run in the
background and can be suspended, for example:
2. Launch the DAO’s coroutines in the background.
To call TaskDao’s insert() method from
TasksViewModel, for example, you’d use the code:
These changes are needed for all data access methods except for ones that
return live data. Room already uses a background thread for methods that
return live data objects, which means you don’t need to make any extra
changes to your code.
Let’s update the code for TaskDao and TasksViewModel so they use
coroutines.
RELAX

The information we’ve given here is everything you need to know to
run data access methods in the background.

methods that don’t use live data with suspend. This means that we need

TasksViewModel needs a view model factory
As you learned in Chapter 11, you add a view model to fragment code by
asking the view model provider to provide one. The view model provider
will return the fragment’s current view model object if one exists, or create
a new one if it doesn’t.
If the view model includes a no-argument constructor, the view model
provider can create an instance of it with no extra help. But if the
constructor has arguments, it needs the assistance of a view model factory.

Let’s go through what happens when the code runs.
What happens when the code runs
The following things happen when the app runs:
1. TasksFragment calls TaskDatabase.getInstance(), which builds
the database if it doesn’t already exist.
It names the database tasks_database, and uses the Task
data class to create a table named task_table. It then returns an
instance of the database.
2. TasksFragment gets the database instance’s TaskDao object,
and uses it to create a TasksViewModelFactory.
The view model provider uses the newly created
TasksViewModelFactory object to create a
TasksViewModel.
3. TasksFragment sets the layout’s viewModel data binding
variable to the TasksViewModel object.
4. The user enters a task name in the layout’s edit text view.
The layout uses data binding to set the view model’s
newTaskName property to this value.
5. The user clicks on the Save Task button.
This uses data binding to call the TaskViewModel object’s
addTask() method.
6. The addTask() method creates a new Task object, and sets its
taskName property to the value of newTaskName.
7. The addTask() method calls TaskDao’s insert() method.
This inserts a record for the Task object’s data into the database
table. The table autogenerates a value for the taskId primary
key.

THERE ARE NO DUMB QUESTIONS
Q: When I tried to enter a task, the app crashed. Any idea why?
A: This can happen if you update the database schema (the entities that
the database contains) without updating the database version number.
Room keeps track of the schema for each version of the database. If it
spots any discrepancies, it adds an error message to the app’s logcat,
and the app crashes.
Q: I see. How do I fix this problem?
A: The simplest fix is to update the database version number in the file
that defines the database (in this case TaskDatabase.kt).
Q: How do I see the app’s logcat?
A: You should see an option for it in Android Studio. Any error
messages get logged here, so it’s a good place to look if you encounter
any errors.
Q: Are there any tools I can use to inspect the database and see
what records and tables it contains?
A: Android Studio includes a helpful Database Inspector you can use
for this purpose. You can find out more here:
https://developer.android.com/studio/inspect/database
Q: What other database features can I use with Room?
A: You can define relationships between tables, create views, migrate
data when you want to upgrade the database, and more. You can find
more details of these features here:
https://developer.android.com/training/data-storage/room
Q: In your layout code, you used "@=" to bind the edit text to the
view model’s property instead of "@". Why?
A: Because we wanted the edit text to update the property’s value, and
not just read its value. Using @= instead of @ enables this.

TasksFragment needs to display records
You’ve now learned how to build an app that inserts records into a Room
database. The next thing we’ll do is update the app so that
TasksFragment displays a list of all the records that have been inserted.
Here’s what the new version of TasksFragment will look like:
To do this, we’ll use TaskDao’s getAll() method to get all of the task
records from the database. We’ll then display them in TasksFragment’s
text view by formatting them as a single String.
Let’s get started.
Use getAll() to get all tasks from the
database
We’ll begin by adding a property named tasks to TasksViewModel,
which will hold a list of all the tasks in the database. We’ll add the tasks to
the property by setting it to TaskDao’s getAll() method like this:
As you may remember, we defined the getAll() method in TaskDao.kt
using the following code:
As you can see, the method returns a LiveData<List<Task>> object:
a live data list of Task objects. This means that TasksViewModel’s
tasks property also has a type of LiveData<List<Task>>:
As the tasks property uses live data, it always includes the user’s latest
changes. If a new task is added to task_table, for example, the tasks
property’s value is automatically updated to include the new record.
Using live data in this way is good news for the Tasks app because we can
use it to display a list of tasks in TasksFragment that’s always up-todate. Before we can do this, however, there’s one more thing we need to
learn about.
A LiveData<List<Task>> is a more complex
type
When we introduced you to data binding, we showed you how to bind a
view to a String or number, including live data values. We were able to
do this because Strings and numbers are simple objects that can easily be
displayed in a text view.
This time, however, the situation is different. The tasks property’s type is
LiveData<List<Task>>, which is much more complex than, say,
LiveData<String>. Because of this, we can’t simply bind the layout’s
text view to the tasks property directly because the text view wouldn’t
know how to display it.
So what’s the solution?
Use Transformations.map() to transform live data
objects
Before we can use data binding to display the user’s tasks, we first need to
transform the LiveData<List<Task>> into something simpler that the
text view knows how to display. To do this, we’ll create a new property
named tasksString, which will hold a LiveData<String> version
of the tasks property.
We’ll create the LiveData<String> using the
Transformations.map() method. This method accepts a LiveData
argument, and a lambda that specifies how the live data object should be
transformed. It then returns a new LiveData object.
To transform the tasks property into a LiveData<String>, for
example, we could use the code:
Transformations.map() observes a LiveData object and transforms it
into another type of LiveData object.
val tasksString = Transformations.map(tasks) {
 tasks -> formatTasks(tasks)
}
where formatTasks() is a method (which we need to write) that
formats the tasks property’s list of tasks as a String. The
Transformations.map() method then wraps this String in a
LiveData object, so that it returns a LiveData<String>.
The Transformations.map() method observes the LiveData
object that’s passed to it, and executes the lambda each time it’s notified of
changes. This means that in the above code, tasksString will
automatically include any new task records the user enters.
Over the next few pages, we’re going to add the tasksString property
to TasksViewModel, and then use data binding to display its value in
TasksFragment’s layout. This approach means that we’ll be able to
display all of the user’s tasks in a text view that always stays up-to-date.
Let’s update the TasksViewModel code
We’ll begin by updating the TasksViewModel code so that it includes
the new tasks and tasksString properties. We’ll also add two
methods—formatTasks() and formatTask()—to help us transform
the LiveData<List<Task>> into a LiveData<String>.
Here’s the full code for TasksViewModel, so update TasksViewModel.kt
so that it includes the changes below (in bold):
We’ll bind the tasksString property to the
layout’s text view
Next, we’ll use data binding to bind the tasks text view in
TasksFragment’s layout to the tasksString property in
TasksViewModel. Here’s the code to do this; update fragment_tasks.xml
to include this change (in bold):
That’s the only change we need to make to fragment_tasks.xml in order for
it to display a String of the user’s tasks. Before we take the app for a test
drive, however, there’s just one more tweak we need to make.
We need to make the layout respond to live data updates
As you already know, the tasksString property is a
LiveData<String>, which means that it automatically includes any
updates that are made to the records held in the database. If the user inserts
a new record, for example, its data gets added to tasksString’s value.
As we’re using data binding to display the value of the tasksString
property in the tasks text view, we need to make sure that the layout gets
notified whenever the tasksString property’s value gets updated.
Doing so means that the text view will display any new records as soon as
they’re inserted.
You’ve already learned how to make a layout respond to live data updates:
by setting the layout’s lifecycle owner in the fragment code like this:
binding.lifecycleOwner = viewLifecycleOwner

go through what happens when the app runs.
THERE ARE NO DUMB QUESTIONS
Q: What other uses does the Transformations.map() method
have?
A: As Transformations.map() gets reevaluated each time it
receives new data, it’s also useful for things like calculations.
Suppose you have a live data rate property, and a calculation that uses
it. You could, say, perform the calculation inside a call to
Transformations.map() so that it’s automatically recalculated
each time the rate property changes.
Q: Do you always display lists as formatted Strings?
A: No. We did so here because it’s a relatively simple way of displaying
a bunch of records. You’ll learn about a better approach in the next
chapter.

Your Android Toolbox
You’ve got Chapter 14 under your belt and now you’ve added the
Room persistence library to your toolbox.
BULLET POINTS
Room is a persistence library that sits on top of SQLite.
Room apps are usually structured using the MVVM
architectural design pattern. MVVM stands for Model-ViewViewModel.
Define any database tables using an annotated data class. Use
@Entity to tell Room to use the class to create a table. Use
@PrimaryKey to define the primary key column. Use
@ColumnInfo to specify column names.
Define any data access methods in an annotated interface. Use
@Dao to tell Room to use its methods for data access. Use
@Insert, @Update, @Delete, and @Query to define the
required data access.
Define the database in an abstract class marked with
@Database.
If you update the database schema, you need to increment the
database version number.
Data access code needs to be run in the background. You can
do this by turning any data access methods that don’t return
live data into coroutines.
You can use the method Transformations.map() to
transform live data objects. The method observes a live data
object, executes a lambda when the object’s value changes, and
returns another live data object.
Chapter 15. Recycler Views:
Reduce, Reuse, Recycle
Lists of data are a key part of most apps.
And in this chapter, we’ll show you how to create one using a recycler
view: a super-flexible way of building a scrollable list. You’ll learn how to
create flexible layouts for your list, including text views, checkboxes, and
more. You’ll find out how to create adapters that squish your data into
the recycler view in whatever way you choose. You’ll discover how to use
card views to give your data a 3D material look. Finally, we’ll show you
how layout managers can completely change the look of your list with just
one or two lines of code. Let’s get recycling…
What the Tasks app currently looks like
In the previous chapter, we built a Tasks app that lets the user enter task
records into a Room database. The app displays the list of records as a
formatted String, and it looks like this:
We decided to display the task records using a formatted String since it
was a relatively quick and basic way of seeing the records that have been
added to the database.
The list, however, looks a little bland. So how can we improve it?
We can turn the list into a recycler view
Instead of displaying the list of tasks as a formatted String, we can
change it up so that it looks like this:
As you can see, each task record uses a text view and a checkbox for its
data, instead of plain text. The items are also arranged on cards in a
scrollable grid.
This kind of list is created using a recycler view. So what’s that?
Why use a recycler view?
A recycler view is a more advanced and flexible way of displaying a list of
data than using a simple formatted String. It buys you the following
benefits:
A rich UI for the list’s items.
Each item is displayed in a layout, so you can use views such as
text views, image views, and checkboxes to display its data.
A flexible way of positioning items.
Recycler views come with layout managers, which let you position
views in a vertical or horizontal list, a grid, or a staggered grid
where items have unequal heights.
You can use it for navigation.
You can make items clickable so that they navigate to another
fragment when clicked.
It’s an efficient way to display large data sets.
Recycler views use a small number of views to give the appearance
of a large collection of views that extend beyond the screen. When
each item scrolls off the screen, it reuses—or recycles—its view
for items that have scrolled onto the screen.
Recycler views get their data from an adapter
Each recycler view you create uses an adapter to display its data. The
adapter uses data from a data source (such as a database), and binds it to
views in the item’s layout. The recycler view then displays the items on the
device screen as a scrollable list.
The data source, adapter, and recycler view fit together like this:
We’re going to add a recycler view to the Tasks app. Let’s go through the
steps we’ll take to do this.
Here’s what we’re going to do
We’ll go through the following steps to add a recycler view to the Tasks
app:
1. Create a recycler view that displays a list of task names.
We’ll begin by creating a basic recycler view that just displays the
name of each task. Keeping this first version relatively simple will
make it easier to see how each part of the recycler view is
constructed, and how they fit together.

…by defining a layout file
You specify how each item in the recycler view should be laid out using a
layout file. The recycler view uses this layout file when it needs to display
each item. If the layout file is comprised of a single text view, for example,
a text view will be displayed for each item in the recycler view’s list.
To create the layout file, highlight the Tasks/app/src/main/res/layout folder
in the project explorer, and then choose File→New→Layout Resource File.

That’s all the code that we need to tell the recycler view how each item
should be laid out. Next, we’ll create the recycler view’s adapter.
The adapter adds data to the recycler view
As we said earlier, when you use a recycler view in your app, you need to
create an adapter for it.
The recycler view’s adapter has two main jobs: to create each of the views
that are visible within the recycler view, and display a piece of data in each
one. For the Tasks app, we need to define an adapter that uses task_item.xml
to create a bunch of text views (one for each task record that’s displayed),
and puts a task name in each one.
An adapter acts as a bridge between a data source and a recycler view.
We’ll build the adapter over the next few pages. Here are the steps we’ll go
through to create it.
1. Specify what kind of data the adapter should work with.
We want the adapter to work with Task data, so we’ll specify that
it uses a List<Task>.
2. Define the adapter’s view holder.
This controls how each view in the item’s layout should be
populated.
3. Inflate each item’s layout.
When the recycler view needs to display each item, we’ll inflate an
instance of task_item.xml for that item.
4. Display each item’s data in the layout.
We’ll do this by adding each Task’s taskName property value to
the layout’s text view.
We’ll begin by creating a file for the adapter.
Create the adapter file
We’re going to create an adapter for the recycler view named
TaskItemAdapter. To do this, highlight the com.hfad.tasks package in
the app/src/main/java folder, then go to File→New→Kotlin Class/File.
Name the file “TaskItemAdapter” and choose the Class option.

This turns the class into an adapter that can be used by a recycler view.

Define the adapter’s view holder
The view holder contains information about how a view in the item’s
layout should be displayed, and its position in the recycler view. You can
think of it as a holder for the root view of the item’s layout—the layout that
specifies how the recycler view should display each item.
In the Tasks app, we want the recycler view to use the layout file
task_item.xml to display task records. This layout’s root view is a
TextView, so we need to define a view holder that works with text views.
You define a view holder by adding an inner class to the adapter file that
extends RecyclerView.ViewHolder. It includes a constructor that
specifies the type of the layout’s root view (in this case TextView). The
adapter’s class definition also needs to be updated to specify the adapter’s
class name.
Here’s the updated code for TaskItemAdapter; update
TaskItemAdapter.kt so that it includes the changes shown here (in bold):
Now that we’ve defined a view holder, we need to specify which layout it
uses by overriding the adapter’s onCreateViewHolder() method.
Override the onCreateViewHolder() method
The adapter’s onCreateViewHolder() method gets called each time
the recycler view requires a new view holder. The recycler view calls the
method repeatedly when it’s first constructed to build the set of view
holders that will be displayed on the screen.
The onCreateViewHolder() method needs to do two things: inflate
the layout that’s used for each item (in this case task_item.xml), and return a
view holder. Here’s the code to do this for TaskItemAdapter, so update
the code for TaskItemAdapter.kt to include the changes shown here (in
bold):
RELAX
Writing adapter code can seem daunting at first, as it needs to do
several different things.
Take your time, keep going, and we know you’ll be fine.
As you can see, we’ve put the code to inflate task_item.xml in a new
inflateFrom() method in TaskItemViewHolder, which the
adapter’s onCreateViewHolder() method calls using:
TaskItemViewHolder.inflateFrom(parent)
This approach passes responsibility for the view holder’s layout to the view
holder instead of inflating the layout in the main body of the adapter code.
Add data to the layout’s view

records in the view holder’s layout. We’ll do this by overriding the
adapter’s onBindViewHolder() method, which gets called whenever
the recycler view needs to display data. It takes two parameters: the view
holder the data needs to be bound to, and the data’s position in the data set.

method, which we’ve added to TaskItemViewHolder. The adapter’s
onBindViewHolder() method then calls this each time it runs. We use
this approach because it makes the view holder responsible for populating

THERE ARE NO DUMB QUESTIONS
Q: Why is it called a recycler view?
A: Because a recycler view reuses—or recycles—its views. This makes
it a very efficient way of displaying a list of data.
Q: You added a view holder inner class to the adapter as well. Can
you remind me what that is?
A: It’s a holder for the root view of each item’s layout. Every recycler
view adapter that you create needs to use a view holder.
Q: Does it have to be an inner class?
A: No. As each view holder is usually tied to a single adapter, it’s often
defined as an inner class, but this isn’t mandatory.
Q: Why did you add inflateFrom() and bind() methods to
the view holder? Couldn’t you have included their code in the
adapter’s onCreateViewHolder() and
onBindViewHolder() methods?
A: We added these methods to the view holder because we wanted to
keep all the code that manages view holders in one place. If we’d left
the code in the main body of the adapter, we wouldn’t have had such a
clear separation of concerns.
This approach is particularly useful when you want to do more than
display a single text view for each item in the recycler view, as you’ll
find out later on.

adapter that we’ve just created.

How to add a recycler view to a layout
You display a recycler view by adding an
<androidx.recyclerview.widget.RecyclerView> element to
a fragment’s layout file. The code looks like this:
The line:
app:layoutManager="androidx.recyclerview.widget.LinearLayoutManag
er"
specifies that the recycler view uses a layout manager, which determines
how the recycler view positions its items. Here, we’re using a linear layout
manager, which means that the recycler view will display its items in a
vertical list with full-length rows.

You’ll find out more about layout managers later in the chapter.

We’ve finished writing all the recycler view code
It took us a while, but we’ve now finished writing all the code that’s needed
to display a list of task names in a recycler view. We did this by:
1. Creating an adapter named TaskItemAdapter
The adapter acts as a bridge between the recycler view and its data
source. In the Tasks app, the data source is a Room database
containing task records.
2. Attaching TaskItemAdapter to a recycler view
We added a recycler view to TasksFragment’s layout, and told
it to use TaskItemAdapter in its Kotlin code.
3. Passing an up-to-date List<Task> to TaskItemAdapter
We did this by making TasksFragment set
TaskItemAdapter’s data property each time
TasksViewModel’s live data list of tasks gets updated.
Before we take the app for a test drive and see what the recycler view looks
like, let’s go through what happens when the code runs.
What happens when the code runs
The following things happen when the app runs:
1. When the app is launched, MainActivity displays
TasksFragment.
TasksFragment uses TasksViewModel as its view model.
2. TasksFragment creates a TaskItemAdapter object, and assigns
it to the recycler view as its adapter.
3. TasksFragment observes TasksViewModel’s tasks property.
This property is a LiveData<List<Task>>, which holds an
up-to-date list of records from the database.
4. TasksFragment sets the TaskItemAdapter’s data property to
the List<Task>.
5. TaskItemAdapter’s onCreateViewHolder() method is called for
each item that needs to be displayed in the recycler view.
This creates a TaskItemViewHolder for each item. A layout
(defined by task_item.xml) is inflated for each view holder.
6. TaskItemAdapter’s onBindViewHolder() is called for each
TaskItemViewHolder.
This binds data to the text view in each view holder’s layout.
7. Each time TasksViewModel’s tasks property gets updated,
TasksFragment passes an updated List<Task> to the
TaskItemAdapter.
Steps 5 to 6 are repeated so the recycler view stays up-to-date.
Let’s take the app for a test drive.
Test Drive
When we run the app, TasksFragment displays the name of each task in
a recycler view.
When we enter new tasks, these get added to the recycler view’s list. The
app works as planned.

Recycler views are very flexible
So far in this chapter, you’ve learned how to build a basic recycler view that
displays a list of task names. You did this by creating a layout that’s used by
each item in the list, defining an adapter to populate it with data, and adding
a recycler view to TaskFragment’s layout.
While recycler views can seem overly complicated, they’re extremely
flexible.
In this app, we created a recycler view to display a simple list of task
names, but this was just to get you started. You can also use recycler views
to do other things, such as:
Displaying a list of images by adding an image view to the
item’s layout
Using a different layout manager to display items in a grid
instead of a vertical list
Making it respond to clicks so that you can use it for
navigation
To show you how flexible recycler views can be, we’re going to change up
the recycler view we just created by getting it to show more information
about each task.
Let’s take a look at what the new version of the recycler view will look like.
Recycler view 2.0
We’re going to update the recycler view so that it shows the name of each
task in a text view, and whether each task has been completed with a
checkbox. We’ll display each task record on a single card, which will look
like it’s slightly elevated. The cards will be arranged in a grid.
Here’s what the new version of the recycler view will look like:
We’re going to create this version of the recycler view by changing
task_item.xml (the layout used by the recycler view’s items) so that it uses a
card view. This is a type of frame layout that has rounded corners and
shadows to make it look as though it’s elevated above its background.

You create a card view by adding an
<androidx.cardview.widget.CardView> element to your layout
code. The code for a typical card view looks like this:
As you can see, the above code includes an extra namespace of:
xmlns:app="http://schemas.android.com/apk/res-auto"
This lets you add attributes that give the card rounded corners and a drop
shadow to make it look higher than its background. You add rounded
corners using the app:cardCornerRadius attribute, and the
app:cardElevation attribute sets its elevation and adds drop shadows:

THERE ARE NO DUMB QUESTIONS
Q: Can I display images inside a recycler view?
A: Yes! To do this, add an image view to the item’s layout, then
populate it in the view holder’s bind() method.
Q: Do recycler views only work with database data?
A: No, they can be used with data from other sources too, just so long
as the adapter has access to the data.
Q: I see that the view holder code uses findViewById(). I
thought we’d stopped using that?
A: The code uses this method to get a reference to each view in the
item’s layout that we want to populate with data. We’ve used this
approach as it’s a relatively simple way of adding data to each view.
In the next chapter, you’ll learn how to replace these calls using data
binding.
Q: Can recycler views use multiple layouts?
A: Yes! You can, say, make the first item in the list have a different
layout to the rest of the items.
To do this, you create each layout you want to use, and define a view
holder for each one. You then override the adapter’s
getItemViewType() method to say which view holder should be
used for which position in the recycler view. Finally, you update
onCreateViewHolder() to check its view type parameter, and
create the correct corresponding view holder.
What the recycler view looks like so far
If we were to run the app after updating the code for task_item.xml and
TaskItemAdapter.kt, we’d see a recycler view that displays tasks in a
vertical list of card views like this:
The recycler view arranges the cards in this way because we specified in
fragment_tasks.xml that it must use a linear layout manager like this:
By default, this layout manager arranges items in a vertical list with fulllength rows. You can, however, include extra options—or use a different
type of layout manager—to change the way in which items are displayed.
Let’s take a look at some of the possible options.
The layout manager gallery
Here are some of the other ways in which you might want to arrange items
in a recycler view, and how to create each one.
Display items in a horizontal row
A linear layout manager displays items in a vertical list by default. You can,
however, display items in a horizontal row instead by changing the
orientation to horizontal:
<androidx.recyclerview.widget.RecyclerView ...

app:layoutManager="androidx.recyclerview.widget.LinearLayoutManag
er"
 android:orientation="horizontal" />
Use GridLayoutManager to display items in a grid
If you want to arrange items in a grid, try using a GridLayoutManager.
Use app:spanCount to specify how many columns the grid should have:
<androidx.recyclerview.widget.RecyclerView ...

app:layoutManager="androidx.recyclerview.widget.GridLayoutManager
"
 app:spanCount="2" />
Arrange items in a staggered grid
If your items have unequal sizes, you can use a
StaggeredGridLayoutManager instead like this:
<androidx.recyclerview.widget.RecyclerView ...

app:layoutManager="androidx.recyclerview.widget.StaggeredGridLayo
utManager"
 app:spanCount="2" />
Let’s apply one of these styles to the Tasks app’s recycler view, and see
what happens when the app runs.
Update fragment_tasks.xml to arrange items
in a grid
We’re going to update the recycler view so that it arranges its items in a
grid with two columns. The updated layout code for this change is shown
below; update fragment_tasks.xml to include these changes (in bold):
What happens when the code runs
The following things happen when the app runs:
1. TasksFragment creates a TaskItemAdapter object, and assigns
it to the recycler view as its adapter.
2. TasksFragment sets the TaskItemAdapter’s data property to a
List<Task>.
TasksFragment gets this List<Task> by observing
TasksViewModel’s tasks property.
3. TaskItemAdapter’s onCreateViewHolder() method is called for
each item that needs to be displayed in the recycler view.
This creates a TaskItemViewHolder for each item. A layout
(defined by task_item.xml) is inflated for each view holder.
4. TaskItemAdapter’s onBindViewHolder() is called for each
TaskItemViewHolder.
This binds data to the views in each view holder’s layout.
5. The recycler view uses its layout manager to arrange its items.
As the recycler view uses a GridLayoutManager with a
spanCount of 2, it arranges items in a grid with two columns.
6. Each time TasksViewModel’s tasks property gets updated,
TasksFragment passes an updated List<Task> to the
TaskItemAdapter.
Steps 3 to 5 are repeated so the recycler view stays up-to-date.

Test Drive
When we run the app, TasksFragment’s recycler view displays a grid of
cards, each one showing a task name and whether the task has been
completed.
When we enter new tasks, these get added to the recycler view as soon as
we click on the Save Task button.
Congratulations! You’ve now learned how to control the recycler view’s
appearance with layout managers, and display data in a scrollable grid of
cards.
In the next chapter, we’ll build on this knowledge to make further
improvements to the recycler view.

BULLET POINTS
A recycler view is a flexible way of displaying a scrollable list
of data.
Add a dependency to the recycler view library in the app’s
build.gradle file.
For each recycler view, you define a layout for its items, and an
adapter that puts data into each item’s views. The recycler view
then displays these items.
Each adapter needs to extend RecyclerView.Adapter.
The adapter uses a view holder that extends
RecyclerView.ViewHolder. It contains information
about how each item’s layout should be displayed, and it’s
usually defined as an inner class of the adapter.
The adapter’s onCreateViewHolder() method inflates
each item’s layout, and creates view holders.
The adapter’s onBindViewHolder() method binds data to
the views in each item’s layout.
Add a recycler view to a layout using the
<androidx.recyclerview.widget.RecyclerView
> element.
A card view is a type of frame layout that has rounded corners
and elevation.
Add a dependency to the card view library in the app’s
build.gradle file.
Use a layout manager to control how items are displayed.
A LinearLayoutManager arranges items in a vertical list
or horizontal row.
A GridLayoutManager arranges items in a grid.
A StaggeredGridLayoutManager is like a
GridLayoutManager except the items can have unequal
sizes.
Chapter 16. Diffutil and Data
Binding: Life in the Fast Lane
Your app needs to run as smoothly and efficiently as possible.
But if you’re not careful, large or complex data sets can cause your recycler
view to glitch. In this chapter, we’ll introduce you to DiffUtil: a utility class
that adds extra smarts to your recycler view. You’ll find out how to use it
to make efficient updates to your recycler view. You’ll discover how
ListAdapters make using DiffUtil a breeze. And along the way, you’ll
learn how to get rid of findViewById() for good by implementing data
binding in your recycler view code.
The recycler view displays task data
correctly…
In the previous chapter, we added a recycler view to the Tasks app that
displays its data exactly how we want it to. Each task is shown in a separate
card, and each card displays the task name and whether it has been
completed. The cards are then laid out in a two-column grid like this:
…but when the data’s updated, the recycler view jumps
Each time we add a new task, the recycler view gets redrawn so that it
includes the new record and stays up-to-date. When it does this, however,
the recycler view jumps and it’s not very smooth.
Each time the recycler view needs to be updated, its entire list gets redrawn.
There are no smooth transitions to indicate what’s changed, and if the list is
very long, the user might lose her place. It’s also inefficient for large data
sets, and can lead to performance problems.
Before we solve these problems, let’s have a quick reminder of how the
Tasks app is structured.
The Tasks app revisited
As we’re sure you remember, the Tasks app lets the user enter task records,
which are stored in a Room database. It includes a recycler view, which
displays all of the records that have been entered.
The app’s main screen is defined by a fragment named TasksFragment,
which uses a view model named TasksViewModel. Its layout—
fragment_tasks.xml—includes a recycler view that displays a grid of tasks.
The recycler view uses an adapter named TaskItemAdapter, and its
items are arranged using the task_item.xml layout file.
Here’s how these parts of the app fit together:
We need to fix TasksFragment’s recycler view so that it no longer
jumps when a new record gets added. In order to do this, let’s revisit how
the recycler view’s data gets set.
How the recycler view gets its data
The following things happen each time the recycler view’s data needs to be
updated:
1. TasksFragment is notified when a record is added to the
database.
This happens because it observes TasksViewModel’s tasks
property: a LiveData<List<Task>> that gets its data from the
database.
2. TasksFragment sets TaskItemAdapter’s data property, which
holds the recycler view’s data.
It sets it to a new List<Task> (that it gets from the tasks
property) that includes the latest record changes.
3. TaskItemAdapter tells the recycler view that the data has
changed.
The recycler view responds by redrawing and rebinding each item
in the list.
The data property’s setter calls
notifyDataSetChanged()
The recycler view redraws and rebinds its entire list because of the setter,
which we added to TaskItemAdapter’s data property. Here’s a
reminder of the code:
The setter gets called each time the data property needs to be updated. As
you can see, it sets the data property to the new value, and then calls
notifyDataSetChanged(). This method tells any observers—
including the recycler view—that the data set has changed, so the recycler
view is redrawn to include the latest changes.
notifyDataSetChanged() redraws the entire list
Using notifyDataSetChanged(), however, is problematic. Each time
it’s called, it tells the recycler view that the data property is out of date in
some way, without specifying how. As the recycler view doesn’t know
what’s changed, it responds by rebinding and redrawing every item in its
list.
When the entire recycler view rebinds and redraws its items in this way, it
loses track of the user’s position in the list. If the list contains more than a
few records, this can cause the list to jump.
It’s also inefficient for large data sets. If the recycler view contains many
items, rebinding and redrawing each item is a lot of unnecessary work and
can lead to performance problems.
Each time notifyDataSetChanged() gets called, the recycler view
rebinds and redraws its entire list. This is inefficient, particularly for
large data sets.
BRAIN POWER
How do you think we could stop the recycler view’s entire list from
being redrawn each time the user enters a new record?
Tell the recycler view what needs to change
A more efficient alternative to calling notifyDataSetChanged() is to
tell the recycler which items in the list have changed so that it only updates
those items. If a new task record gets added to the database, for example,
it’s more efficient for the recycler view to just add that item than it is to
rebind and redraw the entire list.
Manually working out these differences can be tricky and require a lot of
code. The good news, however, is that the recycler view library includes a
utility class named DiffUtil that takes care of all this hard work for you.
DiffUtil works out differences between lists
The DiffUtil class specializes in finding out what the differences are
between two lists so that you don’t have to.
Each time an adapter is given a new version of the list its recycler view
uses, DiffUtil compares it with the old version. It figures out what items
have been added, removed, or updated, and it then tells the recycler view
what needs to be changed in the most efficient way possible:
NOTE
To be precise, it works out what the changes are using Eugene W. Myers’s clever
difference algorithm.
As the recycler view no longer needs to redraw and rebind its entire list,
using DiffUtil is a much more efficient way of updating a recycler
view’s data. It also means that the user doesn’t lose her place in the list, and
the recycler view can even provide smooth transition animations to make it
clear what changes there have been.
Here’s what we’re going to do
In this chapter, we’re going to make improvements to the Tasks app’s
recycler view so that it uses DiffUtil and populates its views using data
binding. These changes will make the recycler view more efficient, and
improve the user’s experience of it too.
Here are the steps we’ll take:
1. Make the recycler view use DiffUtil.
We’ll create a new class named TaskDiffItemCallback that
uses DiffUtil to compare items in a list. We’ll then update the
TaskItemAdapter code so that it uses this new class. These
changes will make the recycler view more efficient, and give the
user a smoother experience when they use it.
2. Implement data binding in the recycler view’s layout.
We’ll remove the calls to findViewById() in the
TaskItemAdapter code, and populate each item’s views using
data binding.
We’ll begin by making the recycler view use DiffUtil.
We need to implement DiffUtil.ItemCallback
In order to use DiffUtil with the Tasks app’s recycler view, we need to
create a new class (which we’ll name TaskDiffItemCallback) that
implements the DiffUtil.ItemCallback abstract class. This class is
used to calculate the difference between two non-null items in a list, and
will help make the recycler view more efficient.
When you implement DiffUtil.ItemCallback, you first need to
specify the type of objects it works with. This is done using generics like
so:
You also need to override two methods: areItemsTheSame() and
areContentsTheSame().
areItemsTheSame() is used to check whether two objects that are
passed to it refer to the same item. We’ll implement it using:
so that if both objects have the same taskId, it means they refer to the
same item, and the method returns true.
areContentsTheSame() is used to check whether the two objects have
the same contents, and it’s only called if areItemsTheSame() is true.
As Task is a data class, we can implement this method using:
Create TaskDiffItemCallback.kt
To create the new class, highlight the com.hfad.tasks package in the
app/src/main/java folder, then go to File→New→Kotlin Class/File. Name
the file “TaskDiffItemCallback” and choose the Class option.
Once you’ve created the file, update its code so that it looks like this:
A ListAdapter accepts a DiffUtil.ItemCallback
argument
Now that we’ve defined TaskDiffItemCallback, we need to use it in
the adapter code. To do this, we’ll update TaskItemAdapter so that it
extends the ListAdapter class instead of RecyclerView.Adapter.
ListAdapter is a type of RecyclerView.Adapter that’s designed
to work with lists. It provides its own backing list so that you don’t have to
define your own, and it accepts a DiffUtil.ItemCallback in its
constructor.
We’re going to specify that TaskItemAdapter is a type of
ListAdapter that provides its own List<Task>, and we’ll pass it an
instance of TaskDiffItemCallback. Here’s the code to do this:
A ListAdapter is a type of RecyclerView.Adapter that provides its own
backing list. It works well with DiffUtil.
We can simplify the rest of TaskItemAdapter’s code
Once TaskItemAdapter has been changed to extend ListAdapter,
we can remove its List<Task> data property, along with its setter.
This property is no longer needed because ListAdapters come with
their own backing list, so you don’t need to define your own.
We can also delete TaskItemAdapter’s getItemCount() method.
This was needed when the adapter extended RecyclerView.Adapter,
but ListAdapter provides its own implementation, so it’s no longer
required.
Finally, we need to update the adapter’s onBindViewHolder() method
so that instead of using:
val item = data[position]
to get the item at a certain position from the data property, it uses:
val item = getItem(position)
This gets the item at the specified position from the adapter’s backing list.

Let’s run through what happens when the app runs.
What happens when the code runs
The following things happen when the app runs:
1. When the app launches, MainActivity displays TasksFragment.
TasksFragment uses TasksViewModel as its view model.
2. TasksFragment creates a TaskItemAdapter object, and assigns
it to the recycler view as its adapter.
3. TasksFragment observes TasksViewModel’s tasks property.
This property is a LiveData<List<Task>> that holds an upto-date list of records from the database.
4. Each time the tasks property gets a new value, TasksFragment
submits its List<Task> to the TaskItemAdapter.
5. TaskItemAdapter uses TaskDiffItemCallback to compare its
old data with the new.
It uses TaskDiffItemCallback’s areItemsTheSame()
and areContentsTheSame() methods to figure out what’s
changed.
6. TaskItemAdapter tells the recycler view what’s changed.
The recycler view rebinds and redraws the necessary items.
Test Drive
When we run the app, TasksFragment displays a grid of cards in the
recycler view as before.
When we enter a new task name and click on the button, a new task card is
added to the recycler view, and the existing cards shuffle along to
accommodate it.
The recycler view behaves in this way because we’re using DiffUtil to
submit changes to it, instead of replacing the entire list.

Recycler views can use data binding
Another way in which we can improve the Tasks app’s recycler view is to
make it use data binding.
As you may recall, TaskItemAdapter’s TaskItemViewHolder
inner class uses findViewById() to get a reference to the views for
each item in the recycler view. The view holder’s bind() method then
uses these references to add data to each view.
Here’s a reminder of this code:
If we change the recycler view to use data binding, we can remove the calls
to findViewById(), and make each view fetch its own data.
How we’ll implement data binding
We’ll make the recycler view use data binding in a similar way to how it’s
implemented with fragments. We’ll go through the following steps:
1. Add a data binding variable to task_item.xml.
We’ll add a <layout> element to the layout’s root, and create a
data binding variable named task whose type is Task. This will
generate a binding class named TaskItemBinding.
2. Set the data binding variable in TaskItemAdapter.
We’ll use TaskItemBinding to inflate each item’s layout, and
set its data binding variable to the Task object for that item.
3. Use the data binding variable to set the views data.
Finally, we’ll update task_item.xml so that each view fetches its
data from the layout’s Task object.
Let’s begin by defining the data binding variable.
Add a data binding variable to task_item.xml
We’ll begin by adding a <layout> element to task_item.xml’s root, and
specifying a data binding variable. Instead of using it to bind views to a
view model, however, we’ll specify that its type is Task.
Here’s the code to do this: update task_item.xml to include these changes (in
bold):
Making task_item.xml’s root element a <layout> tells Android that you
want to use data binding with it, so it generates a new binding class named
TaskItemBinding. We’ll use this class to inflate the above layout, and
set its task data binding variable to a Task object.

holder code

THERE ARE NO DUMB QUESTIONS
Q: Why did we use a RecyclerView.Adapter in the previous
chapter? Could we have used ListAdapter instead?
A: We used a RecyclerView.Adapter in the previous chapter so
that you could get a feel for how recycler views fit together and work.
Using a ListAdapter is an extra refinement that works well with
DiffUtil, which is why we introduced it in this chapter.
Q: Can you use DiffUtil without implementing a
ListAdapter?
A: You can, but it’s more complicated. It’s easier to use
ListAdapter instead.
Q: Why are we replacing those calls to findViewById()?
A: findViewById() has a number of disadvantages, as we
discussed in Chapter 10.
First, it can be quite inefficient. Each time the method’s called, Android
has to search the layout’s hierarchy for a view with a matching ID. If it
doesn’t find one, the app will crash.
findViewById() isn’t type-safe either. If you specify an incorrect
type for the view you want to find, the app may crash when you try to
use it.
Q: Oh yes, I think I remember these disadvantages. And data
binding doesn’t have these problems?
A: No. Using data binding is safer because it means you’re much less
likely to experience these kinds of runtime errors.
Q: In task_item.xml you defined a data binding variable whose type
is Task. Why?
A: Because we wanted to display values from each item’s Task object,
and creating a variable of this type lets us set these values using data
binding.
What happens when the code runs
The following things happen when the app runs:
1. task_item.xml defines a Task data binding variable named
task.
As task_item.xml has a <layout> element at its root, a binding
class named TaskItemBinding is generated for this layout.
2. TasksFragment creates a TaskItemAdapter object, and assigns
it to the recycler view as its adapter.
3. TasksFragment submits a List<Task> to the TaskItemAdapter.
The List<Task> contains an up-to-date list of records from the
database.
4. TaskItemAdapter’s onCreateViewHolder() method is called for
each item that needs to be displayed in the recycler view.
onCreateViewHolder() calls
TaskItemViewHolder.inflateFrom(), which creates a
TaskItemBinding object. It inflates the object’s layout, and
uses it to create a TaskItemViewHolder.
5. TaskItemAdapter’s onBindViewHolder() method is called for
each TaskItemViewHolder.
This calls the TaskItemViewHolder’s bind() method,
which uses the TaskItemBinding object to set the layout’s
task variable to the item’s Task.
6. Data binding code in task_item.xml uses the task property to
set the views for each item.
The task_name view’s text property is set to
task.taskName, and the task_done view’s checked
property is set to task.taskDone.
Test Drive
When we run the app, TasksFragment displays a grid of cards in the
recycler view. It behaves in the same way as before, but this time, we’re
using data binding.
Congratulations! You’ve now learned how to implement data binding with a
recycler view, as well as how to utilize DiffUtil.
In the next chapter, we’ll build on this knowledge to make the recycler view
navigate to individual records.

Your Android Toolbox
You’ve got Chapter 16 under your belt and now you’ve added DiffUtil
and recycler view data binding to your toolbox.
BULLET POINTS
Each time notifyDataSetChanged() is called, the
recycler rebinds and redraws every item.
DiffUtil figures out which items have changed, and updates
the recycler view in the most efficient way possible.
A ListAdapter is a type of RecyclerView.Adapter
that works with lists. It provides its own backing list, and
accepts a DiffUtil.ItemCallback in its constructor.
DiffUtil.ItemCallback calculates the difference
between two non-null items in a list.
Override the DiffUtil.ItemCallback’s
areItemsTheSame() method to specify whether two
objects refer to the same item.
Override the DiffUtil.ItemCallback’s
areContentsTheSame() method to specify whether two
objects have the same contents.
Use the submitList() method to submit a new version of a
list to a ListAdapter.
Recycler views can use data binding.
Define a data binding variable in the layout used for the
recycler view’s items.
Set the data binding variable in the recycler view’s adapter
code.
Chapter 17. Recycler View
Navigation: Pick a Card
Some apps rely on the user selecting an item from a list.
And in this chapter, you’ll learn how to make recycler views a core part
of your app design by making their items clickable. You’ll discover how
to implement recycler view navigation by making the app navigate to a
new screen each time the user clicks on a record. You’ll find out how to
show the user extra information about their chosen record, and update it
in the database. By the end of this chapter, you’ll have all the tools you need
to transform your awesome ideas into the app of your dreams…
Recycler views can be used for navigation
In the previous two chapters, you learned how to build a recycler view that
displays a scrollable list of data, and used DiffUtil to make it more
efficient. But this isn’t the end of the story.
Recycler views are a major component of many Android apps because, in
addition to displaying lists of data, you can use them to navigate through
the app. When the user clicks on an item in the recycler view, you can make
the app navigate to a new fragment, which displays further details of that
record.
To see how this works, we’re going to change up the Tasks app so that
when the user clicks on one of the tasks in its recycler view, it navigates to a
new fragment. This fragment will display the record that’s been selected,
and let the user update or delete it:
How the Tasks app is currently structured
Before we look at how we need to change the Tasks app, let’s remind
ourselves of its current structure.
The app is comprised of a single activity (MainActivity), which
displays a fragment named TasksFragment. This fragment is the app’s
main screen, and its layout includes a recycler view that displays a grid of
tasks. The recycler view uses an adapter named TaskItemAdapter, and
its items are arranged using the layout file.
TasksFragment uses a view model named TasksViewModel. The
view model is responsible for the fragment’s business logic, and gets its
data from a Room database using an interface named TaskDao.
Here’s how these components fit together:
We’re going to make the recycler view
navigate to a new fragment
We’re going to update the Tasks app so that when the user clicks on a task
in the recycler view, it displays a fragment named EditTaskFragment.
Here’s what the new fragment will look like:
As you can see, EditTaskFragment includes an edit text and a
checkbox that let the user edit the task. The edit text displays the name of
the task, and the checkbox shows whether the task has been completed.
The fragment also includes an Update Task button, which, when clicked,
updates the record in the database, and a Delete Task button, which deletes
the record. When either of these are clicked, the app navigates back to
TasksFragment, which displays an updated list of tasks in its recycler
view:
Here’s what we’re going to do
We’re going to build the new version of the app in the following three
stages:
1. Make items in the recycler view respond to clicks.
We’ll update the app so that when we click on a task in the recycler
view, its ID is displayed in a toast.
2. Navigate to EditTaskFragment when an item is clicked.
We’ll create EditTaskFragment, and use the Navigation
component to navigate to it when the user clicks on a task record.
We’ll display the task’s ID in the new fragment.
3. Display the task record in EditTaskFragment, and let the user
update or delete the record.
We’ll create a view model for EditTaskFragment, which will
use the TaskDao interface to interact with the database.
We’ll begin by making the recycler view respond to clicks.
Make each item clickable
The first change we’re going to make to the Tasks app is to make it display
a toast when the user clicks on one of the items in the recycler view.
We can make each item respond to clicks by adding an
OnClickListener to each item’s root view. To do this, we’ll call each
item’s setOnClickListener() method immediately after each item’s
data gets added to its layout.
The best place to add each OnClickListener is in the
TaskItemViewHolder’s bind() method, since this is where the
layout’s data binding variable gets set to a Task item. As you may recall,
the bind() method gets called by TaskItemAdapter’s
onBindViewHolder() method, which fires each time the recycler view
needs to display an item’s data.
Here’s the code to add an OnClickListener to the root view of each
item’s layout; we’ll add this to TaskItemAdapter.kt a few pages ahead:
Now that you know how to add an OnClickListener to each item, let’s
make it display a toast when an item is clicked.
Where should we create the toast?
To display a toast each time an item’s clicked, we could simply add the
following code (in bold) to the view holder’s setOnClickListener()
method:
This approach, however, would mean that we’d be putting code that
describes the app’s behavior in the view holder code. This code is
responsible for binding data to each item’s layout, so it’s not an appropriate
place for us to put this kind of code.
Adding the toast code to the view holder would also make the view holder
code less flexible. It would mean that each time the user clicks on an item,
it could only display a toast, and couldn’t be reused elsewhere.
So what’s the alternative?
We’ll make TasksFragment pass the toast code in a
lambda
An alternative is to define the code each item needs to execute in
TasksFragment, and pass it to TaskItemViewHolder—via
TaskItemAdapter—in a lambda. Doing so means that the fragment
controls what happens when an item is clicked instead of the view holder.
Before we look at the code, let’s run through how it will work.
How the code will work
Here’s what the code we’re going to write will do:
1. TasksFragment will pass a lambda to TaskItemAdapter’s
constructor.
The lambda includes the code to display a toast.
2. When the TaskItemAdapter’s onBindViewHolder() method is
called, it calls the TaskItemViewHolder’s bind() method, and
passes it the lambda.
3. The TaskItemViewHolder adds the lambda to each item’s
OnClickListener.
When the user clicks on each item (a CardView), it executes the
lambda and displays the toast.
So that’s how the code will work.
To implement this, we need to update the TasksFragment,
TaskItemAdapter, and TaskItemViewHolder code. We’ll begin
by updating the TaskItemAdapter and TaskItemViewHolder code
so that the adapter is able to accept the lambda, and pass it to the view
holder.
We’ll show you the code for this on the next page.

constructor, we need to pass one to it in the TasksFragment code where
it gets created.

object in its onCreateView() method, which it then assigns to the

As we want to display a toast each time an item in the recycler view is
clicked, we can update this code so that it passes the following lambda to
TaskItemAdapter’s constructor:
The adapter passes the lambda to TaskItemViewHolder’s bind()
method, which uses the lambda in the OnClickListener code it assigns
to each item’s root view. When the user clicks on an item in the recycler
view, the lambda is executed.

THERE ARE NO DUMB QUESTIONS
Q: In the TaskItemAdapter code, you defined the
clickListener argument’s type as (taskId: Long) ->
Unit. Can you remind me what that means?
A: It means that the argument can be passed a piece of code called a
lambda. The code accepts a Long argument named taskId, and
returns Unit.
Q: I see. So it’s really just a way of passing code to a method?
A: Yes. It means that we can make TasksFragment specify what
code should run, instead of hardcoding it in the TaskItemAdapter
code.
Q: Would adding it to the TaskItemAdapter code really be so
bad?
A: It would make the adapter code less flexible, and the app’s
components wouldn’t have such a clear separation of concerns.
Q: I think I’ve seen other ways of making a recycler view respond
to clicks. Are these ways wrong?
A: There are many different ways of making a recycler view respond to
clicks. We chose this approach because it’s pretty flexible, and easier to
learn than some other methods.
We suggest that you get to grips with the approach we’re using in this
chapter before exploring other approaches.
What happens when the code runs
The following things happen when the app runs:
1. TasksFragment creates a TaskItemAdapter object, and assigns
it to the recycler view as its adapter.
The fragment passes a lambda (named clickListener) to the
adapter, telling it to display a toast when it’s executed.
2. TasksFragment submits a List<Task> to the TaskItemAdapter.
The List<Task> contains an up-to-date list of records from the
database.
3. TaskItemAdapter’s onCreateViewHolder() method is called for
each item that needs to be displayed in the recycler view.
This creates a set of TaskItemViewHolders.
4. TaskItemAdapter’s onBindViewHolder() method is called for
each TaskItemViewHolder.
This calls the TaskItemViewHolder’s bind() method,
passing it the item that was clicked and the clickListener
lambda.
5. The TaskItemViewHolder’s bind() method adds an
OnClickListener to the root view of each view holder’s layout.
In this example, the root view is a CardView.
6. When the user clicks on an item in the recycler view, the
OnClickListener registers the click.
It executes the clickListener lambda, which displays a toast.
Let’s take the app for a test drive.
Test Drive
When we run the app, TasksFragment displays a grid of cards in the
recycler view as before. If we click on one of the tasks, the app displays its
ID in a toast.

events. We’ll use this knowledge to make the app navigate to a new
fragment when an item is clicked.

…and create a navigation graph
We’ll add a navigation graph to the project in the same way that we did for
the other apps we created.
Select the Tasks/app/src/main/res folder in the project explorer, and then
choose File→New→Android Resource File. When prompted, enter a file
name of “nav_graph”, choose a resource type of “Navigation”, and click on
the OK button. This creates a navigation graph named nav_graph.xml.
The navigation graph needs to describe how the user will navigate between
TasksFragment and EditTaskFragment. Here’s how the navigation
needs to work:
1. The app displays TasksFragment.
This is the first fragment the user needs to see, so it needs to be the
navigation graph’s start destination.
2. When the user clicks an item in TasksFragment’s recycler view,
the app navigates to EditTaskFragment.
TasksFragment will pass EditTaskFragment a Long
parameter containing the task ID for the clicked item.
3. When the user clicks a button in EditTaskFragment (which
we’ll add to the fragment later in the chapter), the app
navigates back to TasksFragment.

EditTaskFragment
Each time the user clicks on an item in TasksFragment’s recycler view,

This approach, however, means that we’re adding business logic—deciding
when TasksFragment should navigate to EditTaskFragment—to
the fragment code, instead of adding it to TasksViewModel. As you
learned in Chapter 13, the view model code should make this kind of
decision instead of the fragment.
To address this, we’ll use a similar approach to the one we used for the
Guessing Game app in Chapter 13. We’ll add a new live data property to
TasksViewModel, which will hold the ID for the task the user clicks on.
When this property changes value, TasksFragment will respond by
navigating to EditTaskFragment, passing it the ID.

property marked as private, which means that only TasksViewModel

classes.

onTaskNavigated() will set it to null.
