THERE ARE NO DUMB QUESTIONS
Q: In the EditTaskFragment code, I see that you’re using the
findViewById() method to get a reference to the layout’s text
view. Why aren’t you using view binding or data binding?
A: We’re using findViewById() here because it’s the quickest way
of testing whether the task ID has been successfully passed to
EditTaskFragment.
We’ll update the code to use data binding once we’re sure this version
of the code works.
Q: When I tried to update my Kotlin code, Android Studio told me
that it couldn’t find the TasksFragmentDirections or
EditTaskFragmentArgs classes. Any idea why that might be?
A: These classes are generated by the Safe Args plug-in, so make sure
you’ve updated your build.gradle files to include it. Then double-check
your navigation graph to make sure EditTaskFragment accepts an
argument.
What happens when the code runs
The following things happen when the app runs:
1. TasksFragment creates a TaskItemAdapter object, and assigns
it to the recycler view as its adapter.
The fragment passes a clickListener lambda to the adapter,
telling it to call TasksViewModel’s onTaskClicked()
method when it’s executed.
2. TasksFragment submits a List<Task> to the TaskItemAdapter.
The List<Task> contains an up-to-date list of records from the
database.
3. TaskItemAdapter creates a set of TaskItemViewHolders, and
an OnClickListener is set to each view holder’s root view.
In this example, the root view is a CardView.
4. When the user clicks on a task in the recycler view, the
OnClickListener registers the click, and executes the lambda.
It calls TasksViewModel’s onTaskClicked() method,
which sets its _navigateToTask property to the ID of the task
that was clicked.
5. TasksFragment is notified that TasksViewModel’s
navigateToTask property—which uses _navigateToTask as its
backing property—has been updated.
It navigates to EditTaskFragment, passing it the value of the
navigateToTask property.
6. EditTaskFragment gets the value of the taskId that’s been
passed to it, and displays it in its layout.

We want to use EditTaskFragment to update
task records
We’ve now updated the Tasks app so that when the user clicks on a task in
the recycler view, it navigates to EditTaskFragment, which displays
the task’s ID.
What we really want, however, is for EditTaskFragment to display the
complete task record, and let the user update or delete it in the database.
To do this, we’re going to update EditTaskFragment so that it looks
like this:
The new version of the fragment will work as follows:
1. When the user clicks on a task in the recycler view,
EditTaskFragment displays its details.
It gets the task record from the database, and displays the task
name and whether it has been completed.
2. When the user updates the task’s details and clicks on the
Update Task button, the changes are saved.
It updates the record in the database, and navigates back to
TasksFragment.
3. When the user clicks on the Delete Task button, the task is
deleted.
It deletes the task record from the database, and navigates to
TasksFragment.
For each of these actions, the fragment needs to interact with the app’s
Room database, which means that it needs to use the TaskDao interface
we defined in Chapter 14. Before we update the app, let’s have a quick
reminder of what TaskDao does.
Use TaskDao to interact with database
records
As you learned in Chapter 14, you interact with records held in a Room
database using a DAO interface. The Tasks app, for example, includes a
DAO named TaskDao that lets us interact with task records.
Here’s a reminder of what the TaskDao code looks like:
As you can see, the interface includes methods to get one or more records
from the database, and suspendable coroutines to insert, update, and delete
records in a background thread.
We’ll create a view model to access TaskDao’s methods
We need to use TaskDao’s methods to let EditTaskFragment get a
task record from the database, update its details, or delete it. Instead of
adding the code for this to EditTaskFragment, we’ll create a new view
model (named EditTaskViewModel), which will take care of the
fragment’s business logic and data.EditTaskViewModel will access
TaskDao’s methods, and pass the results to EditTaskFragment.
Let’s go and create EditTaskViewModel.

THERE ARE NO DUMB QUESTIONS
Q: You said that the updateTask() and deleteTask()
methods in EditTaskViewModel use coroutines to update and
delete task records in a background thread. Is this necessary?
A: Yes. If you try to run potentially time-consuming database code like
this in the main thread, you’ll get compiler errors.
Q: Could I add Up button navigation to the app too?
A: Yes! This is a great idea, as it will give the user a simple way of
navigating from EditTaskFragment to TasksFragment without
making any record updates.
We’re not including the code to do this here, but by all means have a go
at adding it yourself. If you need a reminder of how to implement Up
navigation, we suggest referring back to Chapter 8.
What happens when the code runs
The following things happen when the app runs:
1. When the user clicks on a task, TasksFragment navigates to
EditTaskFragment, passing it the task ID.
2. EditTaskFragment gets a reference to its
EditTaskViewModel object.
3. EditTaskViewModel calls the TaskDao object’s get()
method, passing it the task ID.
4. The TaskDao’s get() method returns a LiveData<Task>,
which is assigned to EditTaskViewModel’s task property.
5. When the user clicks on the Update Task button, it calls
EditTaskViewModel’s updateTask() method.
This method uses TaskDao’s update() method to update the
record in the database.
6. When the user clicks on the Delete Task button, it calls
EditTaskViewModel’s deleteTask() method.
This method uses TaskDao’s delete() method to delete the
record.
7. The app navigates to TasksFragment.
Any changes that have been made to the task record are reflected
in the recycler view.
Let’s take the app for a test drive.
Test Drive
When we run the app, TasksFragment displays a grid of cards in the
recycler view as before.
When we click on one of the tasks, the app navigates to
EditTaskFragment, which displays the task’s record.
When we make changes to the task and click on the Update Task button, the
changes are saved to the database and displayed in TasksFragment’s
recycler view.
That’s what happens when we try to update a task. What about when we
delete one?
When we click on a task, the app navigates to EditTaskFragment and
displays the task record as before.
When we click on the Delete Task button, the record is deleted from the
database. When the app navigates to TasksFragment, the record no
longer appears in the recycler view.
Congratulations! You’ve now learned how to build an app that uses a
recycler view to navigate to records, which you can then update or delete.
This technique gives you a powerful and flexible way of organizing the data
in your app.
Your Android Toolbox
You’ve got Chapter 17 under your belt and now you’ve added recycler
view navigation to your toolbox.
BULLET POINTS
You can make a recycler view’s items clickable. You can use
this feature to make the recycler view navigate to another
fragment, which shows details of the clicked item.
You can make a recycler view respond to clicks by adding an
OnClickListener to the root view of the layout it uses for
its items.
You can tell the OnClickListener how to respond to
clicks by passing it a lambda. The lambda will get executed
when the OnClickListener registers a click.
The lambda can be defined in fragment code, and passed to the
OnClickListener via the adapter’s constructor.
Chapter 18. Jetpack Compose:
Compose Yourself
All the UIs you’ve built so far have used views and layout files.
But with Jetpack Compose, that’s not the only option. In this chapter,
we’re going to take a road trip to Composeville, and find out how to build
UIs using Compose components called composables instead of views.
You’ll learn how to use built-in composables such as Text, Image,
TextField, and Button. You’ll discover how to arrange them in Rows and
Columns, and style them using themes. You’ll write and preview your own
composable functions. You’ll even find out how to manage a
composable’s state using MutableState objects. Turn the page, and let’s get
composing…
UI components don’t have to be Views
So far in this book, you’ve learned how to use layout files and views to
build swish, interactive UIs. But while we’ve focused on this approach, it’s
not your only option.
An alternative is to build your UI using Jetpack Compose. Compose is part
of Android Jetpack; it’s an entire toolkit of libraries, tools, and APIs
designed to help you build native UIs with pure Kotlin code.
The great news is that using Jetpack Compose builds on your existing
Android knowledge. You can use view models and live data with Compose,
for example, and even add Compose components to existing UIs.
NOTE
You’ll find out more about this in the next chapter.
We’re going to build a Compose app
In this chapter, we’re going to introduce you to Compose by building a new
Temperature Converter app that converts temperatures from Celsius to
Fahrenheit. Here’s what the app will look like:
As you can see, the app uses several familiar looking components. The
main difference is that it’s written using Compose.
Before we start building the app, see if you can tell what Compose code
does by giving the following exercise a try.

Create a new Compose project
We’re going to create a new Android Studio project that will use Compose
for its UI. Create this project now by selecting the Empty Compose
Activity option:
Choosing this option adds a bunch of Compose libraries and code to your
project. It’s the best type of project to create if you want to build an
Android app from scratch with a Compose UI.
Once you’ve selected the Empty Compose Activity option, click on the
Next button to configure the project.
Configure the project
The next screen should look familiar to you, as it includes the same options
that you’ve used to configure projects throughout this book.
Enter a name of “Temperature Converter” and a package name of
“com.hfad.temperatureconverter” and accept the default save location.
Notice that the language is set to Kotlin, and can’t be updated. Compose
apps can only be created using Kotlin, so you can’t select a different
language.
Choose a minimum SDK of API 21 so that the app will run on most
Android devices. This is the oldest SDK that’s available for this type of
project, because Compose is only compatible with API 21 and above.
Once you’ve selected these options, click on the Finish button.
Compose UIs can only be created using Kotlin.
Compose projects have no layout files
When you create a project using the Empty Compose Activity option,
Android Studio creates a folder structure for you, populated with all the
files the new project needs. The folder structure looks like this:
Many of the files and folders should look familiar to you, as they’re the
same ones that get generated for projects that don’t use Compose. It
includes an activity file named MainActivity.kt, for example, and a String
resource file named strings.xml.
The big difference is that Android Studio doesn’t generate any layout
files for you. This is because Compose projects use activity code to define
the screen’s appearance instead of layouts.
What Compose activity code looks like
When you use Compose, the activity code is responsible for the app’s
behavior and its appearance. For this reason, it looks slightly different from
the activity code you’re used to working with.
Let’s see what basic Compose activity code looks like. Open the package
com.hfad.temperatureconverter in the app/src/main/java folder, and open
the file MainActivity.kt (if it’s not already open). Then replace the code that
Android Studio generated with the code shown here:
Compose activities extend ComponentActivity
As you can see, the above activity doesn’t extend AppCompatActivity:
it uses ComponentActivity instead.
androidx.activity.ComponentActivity is a subclass of
Activity, and it’s used to define a basic activity that uses Compose for
its UI instead of a layout file.
Just like all the other activities you’ve seen, the activity overrides the
onCreate() method. Instead of calling setContentView() to inflate
the activity’s layout, however, it uses setContent(). This is an
extension function that’s used to add Compose components—called
composables—to an activity’s UI so they run when the activity gets
created.
Let’s see how this works by using Compose to add some text to the
activity’s UI..

Text composable to the call to setContent(). You can think of Text
as being the Compose equivalent of a text view. Simply specify the text you
want to show, and the activity displays it.

Use composables in composable functions
A composable function is a function that uses one or more composables to
define part of the UI.
To see how this works, we’ll define a composable function named Hello
that accepts a String argument for the user’s name. When the function
runs—or is composed—it will add the String to a Text composable,
which displays the text in the UI.
The code for the new Hello function looks like this:
As you can see, the function is annotated with @Composable. This
annotation is required for all composable functions. If you omit the
annotation, the code won’t compile.
Call the Hello composable from setContent()
Marking the function with @Composable doesn’t just specify that it uses
composables; it makes the function itself a composable, which you can
use in your code like any other sort of composable.

Most UIs have multiple composables
So far, you’ve seen how to run a single composable, but most of the time,
you’ll want to use multiple composables in the UI, or call the same
composable multiple times. If you wanted the app to say hello twice, for
example, you could run the Hello composable function twice with
different arguments like so:
Hello("friend")
Hello("everyone")
When you have a UI that includes multiple composables, you need to
specify how they should be arranged. If you don’t, Compose will stack the
composables on top of one another like this:
So how do you arrange composables?
You can arrange composables using Row or Column
Most of the time, you’ll probably want to arrange composables in rows or
columns, and Compose includes Row and Column composables that let
you do this. To arrange two Hello composables in a column, for example,
you’d simply add them to a Column composable like this:
Column {
 Hello("friend")
 Hello("everyone")
}
When the code runs, the composables are arranged in a column like this:

THERE ARE NO DUMB QUESTIONS
Q: Do I have to use Compose? Can’t I stick to using views and
layouts?
A: Even though Jetpack Compose is still relatively new, we think it has
a big future. It may even become the default way in which you build
Android apps, so we definitely recommend you learn how to use it.
Q: You said every activity that uses composables needs to extend
ComponentActivity or one of its subclasses. Does
ComponentActivity extend AppCompatActivity?
A: No. This might surprise you, but AppCompatActivity is a
subclass of ComponentActivity, not the other way around.
Q: I see. So does that mean that I can add composables to my
existing activity code?
A: Yes. You can even use composables and views in the same UI.
You’ll find out how this is done in the next chapter.

them within Android Studio without having to load the app onto a device.
You can preview any composable function so long as it doesn’t have any
arguments, and you can even use this technique to preview entire
compositions—UIs made up of composables.
A composition is a UI made up of composables.

Use a Button composable to add a button
You add a button in Compose using a Button composable. Button code
looks like this:
When you use a Button composable, you need to specify two things: its
click behavior, and what should be displayed on the button.
You specify its click behavior using the Button’s onClick argument.
This accepts a lambda that runs each time the user clicks the button.
You say what should be displayed on the Button using a separate lambda

the Button. The above code, for example, passes the Button a Text
composable, so it creates a button with text on it.
Let’s write a ConvertButton composable function

Here’s what the code for the ConvertButton composable function looks

MainActivityContent so that the Button composable gets added to

We need to pass a lambda to ConvertButton
To run ConvertButton from MainActivityContent, we need to
pass it a lambda that specifies what should happen when it’s clicked. The
code needs to look like this:
@Composable
fun MainActivityContent() {
 ...
 ConvertButton {
 //Code that runs when the button is clicked
 }
 ...
}
When ConvertButton is clicked, we want it to update the text displayed
by TemperatureText. If TemperatureText was a view, we could
update its text using code like this:
binding.textView.text = "This is the new text"
While this approach works with views, however, it doesn’t work for
composables. With composables, you need a different approach.
Composables and views work differently.
While views and composables let you display similar looking components
(such as text and buttons), they do this in different ways. A composable
isn’t a type of View, and a View isn’t a type of composable, so to interact
with composables, you need to do things a little differently.
To see how this works, let’s go through what happens when the UI is
composed during composition.
COMPOSITION UP CLOSE
When Compose first builds the UI, it creates a hierarchical tree of all
the composables that get called. When the following code runs, for
example:
Compose builds a tree of composables that looks like this:
Once a composable has been composed, it only gets redrawn—or
recomposed—when its inputs change. TemperatureText, for
example, is defined using the code:
@Composable
fun TemperatureText(celsius: Int) {
 val fahrenheit = (celsius.toDouble()*9/5)+32
 Text("$celsius Celsius is $fahrenheit Fahrenheit")
}
Because TemperatureText accepts a single argument named
celsius, it will only be recomposed when the value of celsius
gets updated. If you want TemperatureText to be redrawn with
new text, you need to update the value of its argument.
We need to change the value of
TemperatureText’s argument
As you’ve just seen, composables are recomposed when the values they
depend on get updated. This means that if we want TemperatureText
to display different text when the user clicks on ConvertButton’s
button, the lambda we pass to ConvertButton needs to update the value
of TemperatureText’s argument.
To do this, we’ll add a new celsius variable to
MainActivityContent, and pass its value to TemperatureText.
When the user clicks on the ConvertButton composable, we’ll get it to
update the value of celsius so that TemperatureText is recomposed.
A composable is recomposed when any of its inputs change their value.
Use remember to store celsius in memory
We’ll define the celsius variable by adding the following code to
MainActivityContent:
This creates an object whose type is MutableState, sets its value to 0,
and stores it in memory. You can think of celsius as working a bit like a
live data object. Each time it gets set to a new value, any composables that
use it are notified, and recomposed.
The object is stored in memory using remember. remember stores an
object when the composable that calls it—in this case
MainActivityContent—is first composed, and forgets it when the
composable is removed from the UI. This can happen when the user rotates
the device, and the activity—including its UI—is destroyed and recreated.
Just like a MutableLiveData object, you set a MutableState
object’s value by updating its value property. To set the value of
celsius to 20 when the user clicks on the ConvertButton
composable, for example, you’d use the following code:
ConvertButton { celsius.value = 20 }
In order for TemperatureText to respond to this value change, its
argument needs to be set to celsius.value like this:
TemperatureText(celsius.value)
Each time celsius.value gets updated, TemperatureText is
recomposed with the new value, which changes the text that’s displayed.
We’ll show you the full code over the next couple pages.
The full code for MainActivity.kt
Here’s the complete code so far for MainActivity.kt; update the file to
include the changes below (in bold):
Let’s run through what happens when the code runs, and take the app for a
test drive.
What happens when the app runs
The following things happen when the app runs:
1. MainActivity launches, and its onCreate() method runs.
It calls setContent(), which runs the
MainActivityContent composable function.
2. MainActivityContent creates a MutableState<Int> variable
named celsius, sets its value to 0, and stores it in memory.
3. MainActivityContent runs the Header, ConvertButton, and
TemperatureText composable functions.
It passes the value of celsius to the TemperatureText
composable function, which converts it to Fahrenheit.
4. The Header, ConvertButton, and TemperatureText composable
functions add an image, a button, and some text to the UI.
5. The user clicks on the ConvertButton composable in the UI.
This sets the value of celsius to 20.
6. TemperatureText is recomposed.
It converts the new value of celsius to Fahrenheit, and displays
the result.
Let’s take the app for a test drive.
Test Drive
When we run the app, MainActivity is displayed. It includes an image,
a button, and some text that gives the value of 0° Celsius in Fahrenheit.
When we click on the Convert button, the text is updated to give the value
of 20° Celsius in Fahrenheit instead.
The next thing we need to do is let the user enter her own temperatures.
We’ll do this after you’ve tried the exercise on the next page.
THERE ARE NO DUMB QUESTIONS
Q: Did you say that remember stores an object in memory?
A: Yes—well remembered!
remember stores an object in memory when the composable that calls
it is first composed, and forgets about it when the composable is
removed from the UI. This can happen when the user rotates the device
screen because Android destroys and recreates the activity, along with
any composables.
Q: Can I persist a composable’s state when the screen is rotated?
A: For simple values, you can use rememberSaveable instead of
remember. This automatically saves any value that can be saved to a
Bundle.
Another option is to use a view model. We’ll show you how to use
composables with a view model in the next chapter.
Q: I’ve seen syntax like: var x by remember { ... } What
does this do?
A: It’s the same as using val x = remember { ... } except
you can replace x.value in your code with x. If you use this
approach, make sure you import
androidx.compose.runtime.getValue and
androidx.compose.runtime.setValue or the code won’t
compile.

ChangeHello. The function displays some text that says “Hello friend”,
and a button that, when clicked, changes the text to “Hello everyone”.
Unfortunately a stray cat ran into the kitchen and dislodged some of the
magnets. Can you piece them back together again?

What happens when the app runs
The following things happen when the app runs:
1. When MainActivityContent runs, it creates a
MutableState<Int> variable named celsius, and a
MutableState<String> named newCelsius.
It sets celsius to 0 and newCelsius to "" and stores both in
memory.
2. MainActivityContent runs the EnterTemperature, Header,
ConvertButton, and TemperatureText composable functions.
It passes the value of celsius to the TemperatureText
composable function, which converts it to Fahrenheit.
3. The Header, EnterTemperature, ConvertButton, and
TemperatureText composable functions add an image, a text
field, a button, and some text to the UI.
4. The user enters a new value into EnterTemperature (in this
example “25”).
EnterTemperature sets newCelsius to this value.
5. The user clicks on the ConvertButton composable.
It converts the value of newCelsius to an Int, and assigns it to
celsius.value.
6. TemperatureText is recomposed.
It converts the new value of celsius to Fahrenheit, and displays
the result.

Add padding to the Column composable
We want to add a gap between the edge of the screen and the app’s
components so that it looks like this:
We’ll do this by adding some padding to the Column composable.
Applying padding to composables has a similar effect to when you apply it
to views; it adds extra space around the component’s edges.
You add padding to a composable using a Modifier. Modifiers let you
decorate or add extra behavior to composables. To add padding of 16dp to a
Column composable, for example, you’d use the code:
Column(modifier = Modifier.padding(16.dp)) {
 ...
}
Modifiers are extremely flexible. You may remember, for example, that
when we wrote the Header composable function, we used a Modifier
to set the Image height and width like so:
Image(
 ...
 modifier = Modifier.height(180.dp).fillMaxWidth()
)
We’ll add padding to the app’s Column composable a few pages ahead.
Before we do that, let’s find out how to center the Button.
You can center composables in Columns or
Rows
If you want to center one or more composables, such as a Button or
Text, you can do so using Columns and Rows. There are a couple
approaches you can take, depending on what you want to do.
Center all of a Column’s content
If you want to align the composables in a Column so they’re all centered
horizontally, you can do so by making the Column as wide as possible,
then setting its horizontalAlignment argument.

But what if you want to center just one of the composables?
Center the content of a single Row
If you want to horizontally center a single composable, you can do so by
placing it in a Row. Simply modify the Row to make it as wide as possible,
then set its horizontalArrangement attribute.

Applying themes: revisited
As you learned in Chapter 8, themes let you give your app a common look
and feel. You can use one to remove the default app bar, for example, or
change the app’s colors.
You already know that you can apply a theme by defining one in the app’s
style resource files, which you then refer to in AndroidManifest.xml. Doing
so applies the theme to the app, including any views. What this doesn’t do,
however, is apply the theme to any composables. To style these, you need to
use a different approach.
How to apply a theme to composables
If you want to apply a theme to a composition, you need to do so using
Kotlin code. The following code, for example, uses MaterialTheme and
Surface composables to apply a theme to MainActivityContent:
MaterialTheme is used to apply the default Material theme to
composables. In this case, it applies it to MainActivityContent and
the composables this function calls, such as TemperatureText and
ConvertButton.
The above code also includes a Surface composable, which is used to
style surfaces. It’s used to apply things like 3D effects and shadows.
If you want to override the default MaterialTheme, you can do so be
defining a new theme in Kotlin code. Android Studio usually adds extra
theme code for you when you choose the option to create a new project
with a Compose activity, so let’s take a look at this code and see what it
does.

BE Compose
The composable function below includes a TextField, a Button, and a
Text. When the user enters a name in the TextField and clicks the
Button, the new name should be displayed in the Text. Your job is to
play like you’re Compose, and say if the fiunction works as intended. If
not, how would you change it?
@Composable
fun ChangeHello() {
 val name = mutableStateOf("friend")
 val newName = mutableStateOf("")
 TextField(
 value = newName.value,
 label = { Text("Enter your name") },
 onValueChange = { name.value = it }
 )
 Button(
 onClick = { name = newName }
 ) {
 Text("Update Hello")
 }
 Text("Hello ${name.value}")
}

The composable function below includes a TextField, a Button, and a
Text. When the user enters a name in the TextField and clicks the
Button, the new name should be displayed in the Text. Your job is to
play like you’re Compose, and say if the function works as intended. If
not, how would you change it?

Code changes are needed to make the function work as intended. We’ve added

Your Android Toolbox
You’ve got Chapter 18 under your belt and now you’ve added Jetpack
Compose to your toolbox.
BULLET POINTS
Jetpack Compose lets you build native UIs using pure Kotlin
code.
The UI is built using composables instead of views and layout
files.
Compose activities extend ComponentActivity.
Any composables run from setContent() will be
displayed to the activity’s UI.
A composable function is a function that uses one or more
composables. It needs to be annotated with @Composable.
You can preview composable functions with no arguments
using the @Preview annotation.
Use Text to display text.
Use Row and Column to arrange composables in rows and
columns.
Use Image to display an image.
Use Button to display a button.
A composable is only recomposed when any values it depends
on are updated.
Use remember to store an object in memory.
Use mutableStateOf() to create a MutableState
object whose values can be updated.
Use TextField to display a text field.
Use a Modifier to decorate a composable or add behavior.
Use Columns and Rows to center composables.
Use MaterialTheme to apply the default Material theme to
composables.
You can override the default theme by creating a new one in
Kotlin code, and applying it to the UI.
Use Surface to style surfaces and apply 3D effects.
Chapter 19. Integrating
Compose with views: Perfect
Harmony
You get the best results when things work together.
So far, you’ve learned how to build a UI using views or composables. But
what if you want to use both? In this chapter, you’ll find out how to get the
best of both worlds by adding composables to a View-based UI. You’ll
discover techniques for making composables work with view models.
You’ll even find out how to make them respond to LiveData updates. By
the end of the chapter, you’ll have all the tools you need to use
composables with views, or even migrate to a pure Compose UI.
You can add composables to View-based UIs
In the previous chapter, you learned how to implement a Compose UI by
building a brand new Temperature Converter app. Instead of adding views
to a layout file, you created the UI by calling composables in the activity’s
Kotlin code.
There are times, however, when you might want to use views and
composables in the same UI. This may be because you want to use
components that are only available as views or composables, for example,
or you want to migrate parts of your app to Compose.
The great news is that you can add composables to a UI that’s defined in a
layout file. We’ll show you how this is done by taking the Guessing Game
app we created earlier in the book, and migrating it to Compose.
The Guessing Game app revisited
As we’re sure you remember, the Guessing Game app lets the user guess
which letters are included in a secret word. She wins the game if she
guesses all the letters, and loses if she runs out of lives.
Here’s what the game currently looks like:
Before we start replacing the app’s views with composables, let’s quickly
review how the app is constructed.
The Guessing Game app structure
The Guessing Game app uses two fragments for its UI: GameFragment
and ResultFragment.
GameFragment is the app’s main screen, which the user interacts with to
play the game. It displays information—such as the number of lives left and
any incorrect guesses the user has made—and lets the user make guesses. It
also includes a button that, when clicked, lets the user immediately finish
the game without making any more guesses.
ResultFragment is displayed when the game is over. It shows the user
if she has won or lost the game, along with the secret word.
The app also include two view models—GameViewModel and
ResultViewModel—which hold the app’s game logic and data, and
maintain its state when the app is rotated. GameFragment uses
GameViewModel, and ResultFragment uses ResultViewModel:
Let’s run through the steps we’ll take to update the app.
Here’s what we’re going to do
We’re going to replace the Guessing Game app’s views with composables
in two main steps:
1. Replace ResultFragment’s views with composables.
We’ll add the Compose libraries to the app’s build.gradle files, and
then add composables to ResultFragment’s layout to replicate
the current views. When we’re satisfied that the composables do
what we want, we’ll remove the views from its UI.
2. Replace GameFragment’s views with composables.
We’ll then go through a similar process for GameFragment.
We’ll add composables to its layout to reproduce its current views,
and when we’re certain that they work in the way we want, we’ll
remove the views from its UI.

code. When the app runs, it displays the layout’s views, and populates the
ComposeView with composables.
A ComposeView is a View that acts as a placeholder for composables. It
lets you use Compose in a View-based UI.

THERE ARE NO DUMB QUESTIONS
Q: So ResultFragment’s views and composables are both using
the same view model. Is that right?
A: Yes. The view model has a property for the game’s result, and the
TextView and Text composable both refer to it.
Q: Do composables usually use a view model?
A: They often do, yes. Just like with a View-based UI, view models
provide a useful way of managing the state of any composables so they
don’t get reset when the user rotates the screen.
Q: You’re passing a view model to ResultFragmentContent as
an argument. Can’t the function just get a reference to the view
model?
A: Composable functions can get a reference to a view model using the
viewModel() function, but at the time of writing, the Compose
library it’s in isn’t stable. You can find out more about this—and other
features—in the appendix.
We need to remove ResultFragment’s
views…
You can remove a fragment or activity’s views in a couple ways:
1. By removing any views you don’t need from the layout file
This approach is useful if the UI contains a mix of views and
composables.
2. By removing the entire layout file
If the UI only includes composables, you can delete the layout file,
and remove any references to it in the activity or fragment’s Kotlin
code.
In the Guessing Game app, we’ve reproduced all of ResultFragment’s
views with composables, so we no longer need any of its original views.
This means that we can delete its layout file fragment_result.xml so that the
UI only includes composables.
…and update ResultFragment.kt
Before we delete the layout file, we first need to remove any references to
its views from ResultFragment.kt, and stop using view binding.
NOTE
View binding gives your activity and fragment code an easier way to access a layout
file’s views. As we’re removing ResultFragment’s layout file, it no longer needs to use
view binding.
We also need to tweak the fragment’s onCreateView() method so that
instead of inflating a layout file, it adds the composables to the fragment’s
UI.
As you learned in Chapter 18, you can do this with an activity by simply
calling setContent() from its onCreate() method like this:
When you’re dealing with a fragment, however, you need a slightly
different approach. To find out what this is and why, let’s revisit the
fragment’s onCreateView() method.
onCreateView() returns the UI’s root view
As you know, a fragment’s onCreateView() method gets called when
an activity needs to display the fragment’s UI.
When the UI is defined with a layout file, code in the onCreateView()
method inflates the layout into a hierarchy of views, and returns the root
view. The root view gets added to the activity’s layout, which displays the
fragment’s UI.
But what if there’s no layout file?
Return a ComposeView for Compose UIs
If the fragment’s UI is just made up of composables and has no layout file,
the onCreateView() method must still return a View?, or the code
won’t compile.
You deal with this by making the method return a ComposeView that
includes all of the UI’s composables. Here’s what the code for this looks
like:
When the activity needs to display the fragment’s UI, it calls the fragment’s
onCreateView() method as before. The method returns a
ComposeView that includes the fragment’s composables, which are then
displayed by the activity.

ResultFragment.kt. We’ll show you the full code over the next few pages.

Let’s go through what happens when we run the app.
What happens when the app runs
The following things happen when the app runs:
1. MainActivity launches and displays GameFragment in its
layout.
2. When the user clicks on the Finish Game button, or wins or
loses the game, the app navigates to ResultFragment and calls
its onCreateView() method.
3. The onCreateView() method creates a ComposeView.
4. The onCreateView() method sets the ComposeView’s content to
ResultFragmentContent.
5. When ResultFragmentContent runs, it calls the ResultText and
NewGameButton composables.
The composables are added to the ComposeView.
6. The onCreateView() method returns the ComposeView to
MainActivity.
7. MainActivity displays the ComposeView in its layout.
The ComposeView’s composables are displayed on the device.

Test Drive
When we run the app, GameFragment is displayed. It includes all its
original views, along with three extra composables that let us make guesses
and finish the game.
When we use the EnterGuess and GuessButton composables to
guess which letters the secret word contains, the app registers each guess. If
we make a correct guess, the letter is added to the secret word display, and
if we make a wrong guess, the number of lives left is updated and the letter
is added to the list of incorrect guesses.
Now that we know the three composables we’ve added work, let’s tackle
the remaining ones.

composable
The next view we’ll reproduce is a TextView that uses live data to display
the user’s incorrect guesses. Each time the user makes a wrong guess, it
gets added to the incorrectGuesses property in the view model, and
the TextView responds by updating the text it displays.
Here’s a reminder of the TextView’s code:
We can replace the TextView with a Text composable so that it displays
the same text, but how do we make sure the text gets updated when the
incorrectGuesses property value changes?
Respond to LiveData using observeAsState()
As you learned in Chapter 18, composables are recomposed when any
State or MutableState objects they depend on get new values. This
doesn’t happen, however, for LiveData objects—like the view model’s
incorrectGuesses property. If you try to use a LivaData object’s
value with a composable, it won’t get redrawn when its value changes; it
will just keep using the object’s initial value, so it won’t stay up-to-date.
If you want to make a composable respond to LiveData updates, you can
do so using the observeAsState() function. This function returns a
State version of a LiveData object so that any composables that
depend on it are recomposed when its value changes.
You use the observeAsState() function with code like this:
This defines a variable (whose type is State) that observes the view
model’s incorrectGuesses LiveData property. It means that when
its value changes, composables can respond.
Let’s put this into practice by reproducing GameFragment’s incorrect
guesses TextView in Compose.
Create an IncorrectGuessesText composable
function
To reproduce the incorrect guesses TextView, we’ll define a new
composable function named IncorrectGuessesText. The function
will accept a GameViewModel argument, observe its
incorrectGuesses property, and use a Text composable to display its
value in the UI. Each time the property’s value gets updated, the Text will
be recomposed and display the updated text.
Here’s what the IncorrectGuessesText function looks like:
As you can see, the Text composable in the above code uses a function
named stringResource() to set its text. This function lets you use

Delete fragment_game.xml
The navigation graph includes a reference to the layout file
fragment_game.xml. We need to remove this before we can remove the
layout file.
Open nav_graph.xml in the app/src/main/res/navigation folder, and remove
the line that references "@layout/fragment_game" from the
GameFragment section like this:
Then right-click on fragment_game.xml in the explorer, choose Refactor,
and select the Safe Delete option. When you click on the OK button and
choose the option to do the refactor, the file is deleted.
We can also switch off data binding
One final change we can make to the Guessing Game app is to disable data
binding. As you may recall, we originally enabled data binding so that
views in fragment_game.xml and fragment_result.xml could interact with
each fragment’s view model. Now that we’ve deleted the layout files, data
binding is no longer required.
To switch off data binding, open the file GuessingGame/app/build.gradle,
and remove the data binding line from the buildFeatures section like
this:
When you’ve done this, click on the Sync Now option to sync the change
with the rest of the project.
Let’s take the app for a final test drive.
Test Drive
When we run the app, GameFragment is displayed. This time, its UI is
just made up of composables. They work exactly how we want them to.
Congratulations! You’ve now learned how to add composables to an
existing View-based UI, and even replace your UI with one that just uses
Compose.
We think Compose has a bright future ahead of it, and you can find out
more about it in the appendix. In the meantime, why not try adding
composables to some of the other apps you’ve built in this book?
Pool Puzzle Solution
Your job is to take code snippets from the pool and place them into the
blank lines in the code below. You may not use the same snippet more than
once, and you won’t need to use all the snippets. Your goal is to write the
code for a fragment named MusicFragment that has no layout file and
uses a composable function named MusicFragmentContent for its UI.
A theme named MusicTheme needs to be applied to the UI, including any
surfaces.
Your Android Toolbox
You’ve got Chapter 19 under your belt and now you’ve added
integrating Jetpack Compose to your toolbox.
BULLET POINTS
Apps can include a mix of View and Compose UIs.
Compose can be used in existing apps so long as the minimum
SDK is 21, and the language is Kotlin.
Before you can use Compose in an existing View-based UI,
you need to add Compose options and libraries to the
build.gradle files.
A ComposeView is a View you can add composables to.
You can think of it as a placeholder for any composables you
want to add to your layout file.
Composables can interact with a view model’s properties and
methods.
Use observeAsState() to make composables respond to
LiveData updates.
Once you’ve replaced an activity or fragment’s views with
composables, you can remove its layout file.

Androidville
We’re sad to see you leave, but there’s nothing like taking what you’ve
learned and putting it to good use. There are still a few more gems for you
in the back of the book and a handy index to study, and then it’s time to take
all these new ideas and put them into practice. Bon voyage!
